It breaks the whole thing.

I had built a family on purpose, after the old life. Chosen, not inherited. People I'd picked off the floor of the world, or who'd picked me off mine. A handful of souls scattered across a handful of cities who would answer the phone at three in the morning and not ask why. That family was the one clean thing I'd made before Clara. And now the system — the thing I'd built to save the woman I loved — was telling me, in the only language it knows, the language of proof, that one of those souls had touched the rot.

I didn't know which one. The system is honest but it is not cruel; it had shown me a resemblance, a kinship of method, a hand that had learned at the same table as the man who'd taken her. It had not given me a name. It had given me a wound and let me find the knife myself.

And here is the thing that shames me, that I'll tell you anyway: my first instinct was not to find out who. My first instinct was to warn them. All of them. Because even now, even with the proof breathing on the screen, the idea that one of mine was dirty felt less likely than the idea that the whole world was lying.

That's love for you. That's the flaw. I have spent my whole life unable to believe the people I love could hurt me, which is exactly why they always could.

So I did the only thing a man like me knows how to do when the ground gives way.

I called my family awake.

Let me tell you about them, quickly, because you're going to need to love them as much as I do, and because in a few minutes one of them is going to break your heart.

There's Ifeoma, in Lagos. She runs the bones of everything I've ever built — the quiet machinery that keeps the lights on, the part nobody thanks until it's gone. She is the most decent person I know and she has never once let me believe I'm a good man, which is its own kind of love. When I met her she was nineteen and furious and ten times smarter than the men paying her a tenth of what they paid each other. I gave her a way out. She gave me a conscience. I'd say we're even but she'd say I still owe her, and she'd be right.

There's Basti, in Marseille. A locksmith now, a smuggler once, a man who can open any door in the old port and tell you a filthy joke while he does it. He has the heart of a golden retriever and the rap sheet of a minor warlord and I trust him with my life because he has, twice, kept it. If the hunt for Clara ever became a thing of boots and streets and locked doors instead of light on a screen, it would become Basti's.

There's Yuri, in Kyiv. A listener. He hears things other people miss in the static of the world — has done since before the war made everyone else learn to. Paranoid to the bone, and the terrible thing about Yuri is that he's usually right. He trusts no one, which is why, of all of them, his trust in me has always felt like the most expensive gift in the room.

And there's Noa.

I'll come to Noa.

I reached for them the way you reach for people when the house is on fire — fast, without grace, not caring how it looks. The system has a way of putting out a call across all of them at once, of asking the family to lend me their eyes without me having to dial four numbers with shaking hands. I leaned on it. I said, in the only words I had: Clara's gone. PHANTOM MERCY took her. I think they had help that's close to us. Look. Please. Look at everything and tell me what you see.

And then I sat in the dark and waited for the people I loved to answer, and tried not to wonder which of them I was really asking, and which of them I was warning.

Ifeoma came first, because Ifeoma is always first, because she never sleeps and pretends it's a discipline rather than a grief.

"You look like hell," she said, and even through a bad connection a thousand miles of dark away, her voice did the thing it always does — it made the room smaller and the night survivable. "When did you last eat?"

"Don't," I said.

"When did you last eat."

"Ifeoma."

A pause. She heard it then — whatever was in my voice. She has known me long enough to hear the difference between I'm in trouble and I'm in the kind of trouble I won't come back from the same. I heard her sit up. I heard a chair scrape on a tiled floor four time zones away.

"Tell me," she said. No softness now. The softness was a door she'd closed so she could carry whatever I was about to hand her.

I told her. Not all of it — not the part about the second signature, not yet, not until I knew. But Clara. The trafficking dressed up as mercy. The convoys that were really routes. The children. The number Clara had carried out of the dark like a body across her shoulders: forty-seven. Forty-seven children, somewhere in the machine, being moved.

Ifeoma was quiet for a long time. When she spoke, her voice had gone very level, very careful, the way it does when she's about to be braver than is good for her.

"Then we find them," she said. "All of them. Tell me where to look."

And she began to look. I felt it more than saw it — the family lending me its eyes, my reach suddenly multiplied, the dark getting one shade less total because someone I trusted had turned a light on in another country. It is a particular feeling, being less alone in a hopeless thing. It is, I think, the closest I come to prayer.

Basti answered with a curse and a yawn and the sound of a woman telling him to take it in the other room.

"Do you know what hour it is," he said.

"I need your eyes on Marseille."

The yawn stopped. Everything about Basti stops when you say Marseille like I said it. The old port is his and he is the old port's, and an insult to one is an insult to both.

"What did they do," he said. Not what's wrong. What did they do. Already on his feet. Already, in his head, walking down to the water in the dark to ask hard men soft questions.

I told him a version. Convoys that came in too clean. Mercy that smelled wrong. A woman, taken, maybe held somewhere south. He didn't ask who the woman was. He could hear who the woman was. Basti has watched me love badly his entire adult life and he knew, the way you know weather, that this time it was different, that this time I'd loved someone who loved me back and now she was gone.

"I'll start asking," he said. "Quietly. The way you taught me." A beat. "We'll get her, brother."

He didn't know if that was true. Neither did I. But there are lies you tell each other at three in the morning that aren't lies so much as promises you're both agreeing to try to keep, and that was one, and I held it like a coal.

Yuri took the longest, and answered the way Yuri always does, with a question instead of a hello.

"Who else have you told?"

That's Yuri. The first thing he wants to know is the shape of the circle, who's in it, where it leaks. I told him. Ifeoma. Basti. Him.

"And the fourth," he said. Because Yuri counts my family better than I do. Because Yuri has never trusted the fourth, and has never said so, and has, in not saying so, said everything.

"Not yet," I said.

A silence with weight in it. Then, low: "Be careful who you wake up. Whatever this is, it's old, and it's careful, and careful things don't get careful by accident. Someone taught it to hide." He didn't know how close he'd cut to the bone. Or maybe he did. With Yuri you never know how much he knows and how much he's just afraid of, and the maddening thing is they're so often the same number.

"I'll be careful," I said.

"You won't," he said, almost gently. "You never are, with people. It's the best thing about you and it's going to get you killed." And then, before I could answer: "I'm looking. Give me the threads."

I gave him the threads. The family's eyes, wider now. Lagos, Marseille, Kyiv — three lights in the dark, three people I'd have died for turning their whole attention on the thing that had taken the woman I'd actually have died for. By morning, with their eyes added to the system's, the dark might shrink to something I could put my hands around. The whole impossible country might narrow to a street.

If one of them was clean. If all of them were.

Which left Noa.

I told you I'd come to Noa.

Noa is the ex who still answers. That's the short version and it's a coward's version. The long version is that Noa is the love I broke before I knew what I was for — the woman who got the version of me that gave everything and kept nothing, the version that scattered, the version that fell so hard he forgot to stand. I loved her the way I love, completely, and then I loved someone else the same way, while she watched, because that was the disease and I hadn't found the cure yet. She had every right to hang up on me forever.

She never has.

That's the part I can't explain and have stopped trying to. Noa, in Tel Aviv, picks up. After everything. She picks up like a woman keeping a vigil at a grave she's too stubborn to admit is a grave. Sometimes I think she's the ghost of my flaw, kept alive to haunt me. Sometimes I think she might be the only one who could ever forgive it, which is its own kind of haunting.

I almost didn't call her. Yuri's voice was in my ear — be careful who you wake up — and a small ugly part of me, the part the system had infected with proof, whispered that I didn't know, that I couldn't know, that the second hand could be any of them, even her, especially her, because who better to hide in plain sight than the one I'd never suspect because I'd already broken her?

I hated that thought. I called her anyway. Because if I started choosing who to trust by who I was afraid to lose, I'd have already lost.

She answered on the second ring. Of course she did.

"It's late even for you," she said. Her voice in the dark. There are voices that are also places, and hers is a place I lived once and was happy in and ruined.

"Noa." That was all I had. Her name. The whole apology I'd never finished, folded into two syllables.

She heard it. She always hears it. "What's wrong," she said, and the what's wrong was so tender, so without grudge, that for a second I couldn't speak at all.

So I told her. Clara. I told Noa about Clara — the woman who came after her, the woman who was real, the words coming out clumsy and cruel and I couldn't stop them. And Noa, who had every reason on earth to put the phone down, did not put the phone down. She got quiet, the good quiet, the listening quiet. And then she said the thing that undid me, the thing only she would say:

"Then we get her back. Whoever she is. If she's the one who finally made you stay — then she's family too, and we get her back." A breath. "Tell me what you need."

I closed my eyes. The rain. The radiator. Three lights in three cities and now a fourth, the one I least deserved, lit and turned toward me in the dark.

"I need your eyes," I said. "I need you to look at something with me. There's a — there's a pattern in how they hide. A hand. I think someone close to us might have —" My throat closed. I made myself say it. "I think one of ours might have touched this. I don't know who. I'm afraid to know who. But you're the one I'm not afraid to ask, so I'm asking. Look with me. Tell me what you —"

"Wait," she said.

Just that. Wait. The way you say it when something has moved at the edge of your sight. I heard her shift. I heard her go very still, the kind of still that has its own sound.

"There's something in my—" she started, and her voice changed, dropped, sharpened to a point. "Hold on. There's something here that shouldn't—"

And then she said one more word. A single word, started and never finished, the front half of it landing clear and the back half falling off the edge of the world.

The same word Clara had said. The very last word, the one Clara's message had broken off on, the one I'd read until it wore smooth and never understood.

Noa said it — and then there was nothing.

No click. No goodbye. No line gone dead with the decency of a sound.

Just her, mid-breath, mid-syllable, cut off on the exact word Clara had — and then the silence where her voice used to be.

Chapter 8: Her Shadow Still Moves

The line had gone dead on a single word.

I sat there with the silence pressed against my ear like a hand, and I understood, in a way that had nothing to do with thinking, that I had just heard something I would never be able to un-hear. One of my family — one of the people I had spent years choosing, trusting, calling at the worst hours of my life — had stopped mid-sentence. Cut off clean. On the exact word Clara had been saying the last time the world swallowed her whole.

I won't tell you which word. Not yet. Some things you have to be ready for, and I wasn't, that night, so neither are you.

I called back. Nothing. I called again. The kind of nothing that isn't a phone failing — the kind that's a door closing on the other side of the planet, quietly, by someone who doesn't want you to hear the latch.

And here is the thing about a man like me, the thing I'm not proud of: fear doesn't stop me. Fear is fuel. It poured into me cold and clean and I felt myself go very still, the way I used to go still before I broke into somewhere I had no business being. Except I don't break in anymore. I build. I protect. I find.

So I wiped my face with the heel of my hand, and I went back to the screen, and I asked the thing I'd made to do the one thing I had never quite dared ask it.

I asked it to find a ghost.

Let me tell you how I'd been living, because by now you should know what kind of ruin a man can keep upright on coffee and grief.

Five days into the build. Five days in the Brussels apartment with the radiator that ticked like a patient heart and the window that looked out on nobody. I had stopped counting meals. I'd started counting heartbeats — not mine, hers, the imaginary ones, the ones I told myself were still happening somewhere south of me, in a room I couldn't picture, behind a face I'd helped train.

I had built the system to watch. That's the part I can tell you in plain words without lying. Not to spy — to notice. To learn what the world looks like when nothing is wrong, so completely, so patiently, that the second something is wrong, it feels it the way you feel a draft in a sealed room. The way a mother wakes a half-second before the baby cries. It learns the ordinary so well that the extraordinary lights up like a flare.

I had taught it the ordinary of an entire network of evil. The rhythm of the convoys that weren't really convoys. The breath of the money that went in clean and came out filthy. The hours those men kept, the silences they kept, the patient machinery of a thing that hid children behind the word mercy.

I knew the ordinary now. I knew it cold.

So when I asked it to find a ghost, what I was really asking was simpler, and crueler, and more human than any machine has a right to be.

Show me what shouldn't be moving.

Here is what you have to understand about the dead, and the taken, and the people the world has decided to forget.

They go still.

That's the horror of it and, sometimes, the mercy. When a person vanishes into a place like the one that had Clara, they stop leaving a wake. No footprints. No ripple. The water closes over the spot where they went down and lies flat, and the men who put them there count on exactly that — on the flatness, on the silence, on a world too busy buttering its bread to notice one more dent in one more bed.

The system knew that flatness. It had drawn the shape of it for me, the great still surface of PHANTOM MERCY, every part of it locked into its dead and patient rhythm.

And somewhere in all that flatness, something was moving that shouldn't have been.

I almost missed it. I want to be honest — I missed it. I'm the genius in this story and I looked right past it, because I was looking for a person, for a name, for a face, for the loud obvious thing. The system wasn't looking for any of that. It doesn't get tired and it doesn't get hopeful and it doesn't, God help it, fall in love. It was looking for the one thing in all that stillness that kept twitching — small, faint, stubborn, wrong. A pulse in a corpse. A held breath under a song.

Something in the dead machinery of that network was not dead.

Something was reaching.

I leaned in so close to the screen my breath fogged it.

I won't bore you with what it looked like. I'll tell you what it felt like, because that's the only honest currency I have left.

It felt like standing in a graveyard at three in the morning, the whole field of the dead lying flat and quiet under the frost — and seeing, far off, in the dark, the smallest possible movement. A hand. Pushing up. Pushing through. Refusing.

It felt like that.

Whatever it was, it was patient. It was careful — the way a person is careful when one wrong move means the people watching them will know they're not as broken as they're pretending to be. It moved in tiny increments, at the edges of the network's own machinery, in the seams nobody guards because nobody believes anyone could survive there long enough to use them. It moved the way someone moves a stolen pen under a prison blanket. Slow. Deliberate. Alive.

And it had a manner to it. A way of doing things. A signature, the way the slant of a hand is a signature, the way the rhythm of someone's footsteps on the stairs is a signature even in a house full of strangers.

I knew that manner.

I had loved that manner across a bar in Las Vegas that smelled of spilled bourbon and the rest of my life. I had watched that manner take the world apart and put it back together more honest than it found it. Anyone can lie to you, she'd said, that first night, leaning close enough that I forgot the names of every other woman I'd ever ruined myself over. Proof can't. Build me something that only believes proof.

I built her that thing. And now that thing was looking at a movement in the dead heart of the network that had taken her, and it was telling me, in the only language it has, which is the language of here is what is true —

That the careful, stubborn, impossible thing still moving in the dark, refusing to go flat, refusing to go quiet, leaving the faintest wake at the edges where no one was supposed to be able to survive —

was her.

Her manner. Her care. Her stubbornness aimed like a blade.

Clara was still moving.

I don't have the words for what that did to me, so I'll tell you what I did instead, which is: nothing. For a long time. I sat in the ticking dark of that apartment and I did not move and I did not breathe and the cold went out of me and something else came in, something I hadn't felt in years, something that frightened me more than the fear had.

Hope.

Hope is heavier than grief. Nobody tells you that. Grief you can carry, because grief asks nothing of you, grief just sits on your chest and lets you lie there. Hope makes you get up. Hope makes you believe you can do the impossible thing, and then it stands behind you and watches to see if you'll fail her.

She was alive. Not a memory I was nursing in the dark. Not a heartbeat I was inventing to keep myself upright. Alive, and not just alive — working. Reaching. From inside a place built to make people go still, Clara had refused to go still. She was doing something in there. She was leaving a wake on purpose, because she knew — she knew, the magnificent, infuriating, self-destructive love of my life — that somewhere there might be one idiot left in the world who had built a thing careful enough to feel it.

She'd let herself be taken.

I understood it all at once, sitting there, and the understanding nearly broke me in half. Someone has to protect the children. She'd said it to me once, lightly, the way you say a thing you've already decided to die for so the other person won't argue. I'd thought it was bravado. It wasn't bravado. She had walked into the dark on purpose, carrying everything she knew, and she was holding it together in there, keeping it safe, keeping it provable, leaving me a wake to follow like a woman dropping bread through a forest she already knew she might not walk out of.

You don't deserve a person like that. I certainly didn't. I'd spent my whole life proving I didn't deserve much of anything that didn't have a lock on it.

But she'd left the wake for me. And I was going to follow it if it killed me, and I had a growing suspicion that it would.

My phone buzzed against the desk and I nearly came out of my skin.

Not the silent one. Not the family member who'd been cut off mid-word — that line was still a closed door, still a latch I couldn't reach. This was Yuri, in Kyiv, the most paranoid man I have ever loved, the one who is usually right and hates it. Something is wrong with the picture, he'd written, in the clipped way of a man who's slept four hours in three days. Too much noise tonight. Whole sky lighting up. Somebody wants you looking at the wrong thing.

I read it twice. I felt the old animal in me lift its head.

Because Yuri was right, and I'd been too drunk on hope to feel it. The night was too loud. Somewhere out at the edges of everything, a great clatter was starting up, the kind of clamor men make when they want all the eyes in the room turned one way so that, behind everyone's back, a single quiet hand can do the thing that actually matters. A distraction has a manner too. A signature. I knew that one as well — I'd built a few in the buried life, loud ugly things to keep the guards looking left while you went right.

Someone was about to make a great deal of noise.

And I had a cold, certain feeling it was meant for me — that the second I'd found her wake, something on the other side had felt me find it, and was already reaching for the lights, already raising its voice, already building the storm that would try to pull my eyes off the one warm spot in the dark.

Not tonight, I told it, whoever it was, the Surgeon or worse. Make all the noise you want. I've found her. I'm not looking away.

I texted Yuri back two words — I know — and turned the phone face-down, and went back to the screen with hands that weren't entirely steady, and I asked it to follow her.

Not to her — I knew it couldn't take me to her, not yet, not that simply. But the small careful movements she was making in the dark, the reaching, the leaving of her wake — all of it pointed somewhere. She wasn't just twitching at random. She was depositing something. Slipping something out through the seams, a little at a time, into a place she'd chosen. A hiding spot. A drop. The oldest trick in the world and still the best, because it doesn't need a wire or a signal or a single thing that the men watching her could see: a place where you leave a message for one person, and trust the world to keep your secret until that person comes.

She'd left me one of those once before, you'll remember. Reached forward through time and put her fingerprints somewhere I thought was sealed.

Now she was doing it again, in real time, from inside hell, and the system was watching the faint repeating shape of where her stubborn careful movements kept pointing — the same spot, again and again, the way a hand keeps returning to a hidden pocket to make sure the thing is still there.

It narrowed. God, I watched it narrow. The whole flat dead surface of that network, and out of it the system pulled one warm spot, one place her shadow kept returning to, the way you'd find a single warm stone in a field of cold ones if you only had patience enough and love enough to keep your hand on every one.

A drop. A dead drop. Hers. Waiting.

She had built me a door, and left it where only I would think to look, and now the thing I'd made had found it in the dark.

I want to tell you I was a professional about it. I want to tell you I documented and verified and sealed it with proof, the way she taught me, the way I'd sworn I always would. I did all of that. Later. In that first moment I just put my hand flat against the screen like an animal, like a fool, like a man who has not touched the person he loves in far too long and has just been told the lights in her window are still on.

I'm coming, I told the warm spot in the dark. Hold on. I'm coming.

The drop was sealed.

Of course it was sealed. She was Clara. She would no more leave a thing unlocked than leave a child unguarded. The door was right there in front of me, found, real, hers — and shut, with a lock on it, the way she locked everything that mattered, the way she'd locked her own heart for a long time before she let me near it.

A lock needs a key. And this one needed a key only I would have.

I knew that the way I knew my own name. She hadn't built this door for the system, or for a court, or for the men who'd taken her, or even for the cause she'd burned herself down for. She'd built it for me. The lock would only ever yield to a thing the two of us shared and no one else in the world could know — a number, a thing, a date that lived in exactly two heads on this earth, one of them four hours south in a room I still couldn't see.

And I knew it.

It wasn't her birthday. It wasn't mine. It wasn't anything a file could find or a man could guess or torture out of either of us, because it wasn't written anywhere except inside the two of us, in the soft secret place you keep the moment your whole life turned over without asking your permission.

It was the day we met. The bar. The bourbon. The rest of my life walking in wearing the wrong shoes and the right eyes. Build me something that only believes proof. The day the loud reckless ruin of a man I'd been looked across a crowded room and, for the first time, went quiet.

The key was the date they first met.

I typed it with a shaking hand.

It opened.

Chapter 9: The Diversion

The dead drop opened at 3:11 in the morning, and for exactly nineteen seconds I was the happiest man alive.

I had typed the date with a shaking hand — the day we met, the only password she would ever have trusted me to remember — and the little locked thing she'd left me unfolded like a flower in the dark. I didn't read it yet. I just sat there, breathing, the way you breathe when a doctor says the word benign. She had left me something. She had known I would come. Across all that distance, across whatever room they were keeping her in, she had reached out one hand in the dark and left it open for mine to find.

Nineteen seconds.

Then every screen I owned turned red.

I have been in a lot of bad rooms in my life. I have been on the wrong end of men with guns and the wrong end of women with reasons, and I once spent a memorable forty minutes locked in a meat freezer in Antwerp by a man who, in fairness, had warned me. So I know the feeling of the floor tilting. I know what it is to have the ground decide, all at once, that it is no longer on your side.

This was that. The red was that.

Somewhere out in the dark, far away and very close, the thing I had built — the thing that was my eyes and my ears and my sixth sense, the thing that listened to a whole country's silence and found her heartbeat in it — was being set on fire.

I don't mean a real fire. There was no smoke in the Brussels apartment, just the rain and the radiator and the cold sweat starting between my shoulder blades. But it was a fire all the same. Someone had thrown a torch into the house I'd built to find her, and the house was screaming.

I want to tell you what that's like without telling you how it works, because the how doesn't matter and would only bore you, and because in that moment I didn't care how. I cared that it was happening. Imagine you're a lighthouse keeper, and your whole job, your whole reason for existing, is to keep one light burning so a ship can find its way home in the storm. And imagine someone starts a fire in your lighthouse — not to burn it down, not at first, but to fill it with smoke. To blind you. To make so much noise and heat and panic that you forget about the light, that you drop everything and run for the buckets, and while you're choking and shouting and fighting the flames, the ship slips past in the dark and is gone.

That's what it was. A diversion. A fire set to make me look away.

And the worst part — the part that made my hands go cold around the coffee cup — was that it worked, for a second. Because the first thing the fire did was reach for the one place I couldn't bear to lose.

It reached for the message Clara had left me.

There's a thing they do, the people who hurt the world. The polished ones. The ones with budgets and patience. They don't just attack you. They attack you while you're reaching for what you love. They wait until your hand is out, until you're vulnerable and soft and human, and then they kick.

The Surgeon had done it. I knew it was him the way you know a smell from childhood. The timing was too clean, too cruel, too surgical. He hadn't set the fire when I was hunting him. He'd set it the instant the dead drop opened — the instant I had something to protect. He'd been watching. He'd been waiting for me to find her hand in the dark, just so he could try to take it.

That's a particular kind of cruelty, and I'll be honest with you: for one ugly second it didn't make me afraid. It made me want to kill him.

I have told you about that want. It lives in me. It is the old life, the buried life, the fast and dirty and satisfying life, and it never fully dies, it just sleeps, and it has very light sleep. In that second it woke all the way up and stretched and said, in its reasonable voice: He's right there. He left the door open setting this fire. Reach across and burn his whole world down. Make him feel it. You can do it in your sleep.

And I could have. God help me, I could have.

But Clara built me to believe proof. And a man fighting a fire does not get to also be an arsonist. So I put the want back in its cage — not gently, I never can — and I did the only thing that was left.

I held the line.

Here is what holding the line looks like, told as a feeling, because that's the only true way to tell it.

You are alone in a small room at three in the morning and the whole world is on fire and there is nobody coming to help. You cannot call the fire department. There is no fire department for this. There is just you, and the thing you built, and the people who love you who are all asleep in time zones where the sun is up and the day is ordinary, and you are not going to wake them, because if you wake them you bring them into the fire too, and you swore you wouldn't.

So you sit very still. You stop panicking — not because you feel calm, you don't, you feel like a man drowning in a phone booth, but because panic is exactly what the fire wants. The fire is made of panic. It is shouting at you, Look here! No, here! No, over HERE! — a hundred alarms going off at once, every one of them screaming that something precious is being destroyed, and you understand, slowly, coldly, that ninety-nine of those alarms are lies. That's the whole trick of it. Drown the one real signal in a hundred fake ones. Make the truth impossible to hear.

So you stop listening for the alarms. You start listening for her.

I built it to find her heartbeat in the silence of a country. Now I asked it to find one true thing in a roomful of liars. Find the one alarm that isn't shouting. Find the quiet thing under all the noise. Hold the door she left open. Hold it shut against everyone else and open for her, and do not, do not, do not let go.

I leaned my forehead against the cool glass of the laptop screen, like a man praying at a window, and I said it out loud to an empty room.

"Hold the line."

And the thing I built — the thing a machine had helped me build, in seven sleepless days, for exactly this, for her — held the line.

I won't pretend it was clean.

It was the longest forty minutes of my life and I aged a year in every one of them. The fire pushed and the line bent and there were moments — three of them, I counted, I'll remember them when I'm old — where I thought I'd lost her message entirely, where the smoke filled everything and the open door she'd left me seemed to vanish into the roar, and a sound came out of me that I'm not proud of and won't describe.

But here's the thing about a fire set to distract you: it tells on the man who set it.

You can't shout look away without revealing that there's something you don't want looked at. You can't blind a man without standing in his light. The Surgeon set his fire to make me drop the thread — and in setting it, his hand was all over it, that scarless surgical hand I'd have known anywhere, the hand I'd helped to shape in a life I'd buried. He was so busy making me look away that he forgot I'd built the whole thing to remember a touch. Every time he pushed the fire toward Clara's open door, he left a fingerprint on the door.

I stopped fighting the flames. I started photographing them. Not running for buckets — standing in the middle of it, soaking it all in, every cruel clever push, and letting the thing I built do what Clara made it for: turn it all into proof. Sealed, stamped, court-cold proof that couldn't be charmed or bought or burned. He thought he was blinding me. He was signing a confession in front of a witness who never blinks.

Do it right, I told myself, in her voice, because I'd lost my own again. He wants you to do it fast. So do it right. Make him hate how right you do it.

There was a moment, near the end, when I almost called Basti.

Basti is my man in Marseille — a locksmith now, a smuggler once, a friend always, a big laughing bear of a man who has opened more doors that didn't belong to him than any honest person should admit knowing. If anyone could put boots on the ground in the city where she'd vanished, where the fire seemed to be blowing in from, it was him. My thumb was already on his name. The phone was already warm in my hand.

And then I thought of the meat freezer in Antwerp, and the man who'd warned me, and how I'd walked in anyway because I never could leave a door alone. I thought of all the people I'd loved into the line of fire because I couldn't bear to be afraid by myself. And I put the phone down, third time that week, because waking Basti meant pointing the fire at someone I loved, and I had sworn — to her, to the empty room, to the man I was trying to become — that this time the cost would land on me and only me until I knew exactly what I was dragging people into.

It cost me. It always costs me. But I put it down.

The fire began to fail. Not because I beat it — you don't beat a fire like that, you outlast it, you make holding the line cost him more than the fire is worth. Slowly, sullenly, the alarms went quiet, one by one, the lies running out of air. The smoke thinned. And the door — her door, the one she'd left open for me in the dark — was still open. Bent. Scorched at the edges. Held.

And in the holding, I noticed something I'd remember later as the first loose thread of everything that came after.

The fire was too good. Too practised. You don't learn to set a fire like that overnight — to know exactly when a man's hand will be out, exactly which door he'll be reaching through, exactly how long he can hold a line before he breaks. You learn it by watching. For a long time. By living in the walls of a house long before you decide to burn it. The Surgeon hadn't found me last night. He'd been in my walls for years, patient as rot, and the fire wasn't the beginning of anything. It was the moment the rot finally moved. Whatever PHANTOM MERCY was, it had roots that went down further and older than I'd let myself imagine, and I had the cold sense, standing in the smoke, that I was about to start pulling on one and find no bottom.

But that was a fear for tomorrow.

I had held the line. The light was still burning. The ship had not slipped past.

I sat back. My shirt was wet through. My hands wouldn't quite stop. And I started, finally, to laugh — that terrible cracked laugh that comes out of a man who has been afraid for too long and has nowhere left to put it — because I'd done it, I'd held it, and somewhere out there the Surgeon was learning that his old brother didn't break the way he used to.

And that's exactly when it landed.

In the centre of it all — in the eye of the dead fire, in the quiet after the storm, through the one door that hadn't closed — a message arrived.

Not the old dead drop. Not the thing she'd left me weeks ago and locked with the day we met. Something new. Something now. It came in while the smoke was still clearing, threaded right through the middle of the chaos, as if it had used the Surgeon's own fire for cover — as if someone had waited for exactly this storm to slip a note under exactly this door.

My laugh died in my throat.

Three lines. That's all it was. Three short lines in the dark, and the whole room went silent around them, the rain and the radiator and my own heartbeat, all of it, gone.

They know you opened it.

Stop looking, or the children pay for it.

This is your only warning.

I read it once. I read it again. I read it a third time, the way I read everything that matters, until the words wore smooth.

A threat. Plain and cold. Stop looking. Of course it was a threat — the fire had failed to blind me, so now they'd try to scare me, and a man who hurts children does not bluff about hurting children. Every nerve in my body understood it. Walk away. Walk away or they pay.

I should have been afraid. I was afraid. But that's not what stopped my heart.

What stopped my heart was the last thing on the message. After the three lines. After the threat. Down in the corner, small and quiet, the way you sign something you mean — a single mark. A signature I had read a hundred times on a hundred messages in a better life. The one she used instead of her name, because she said names could be stolen but a manner couldn't, the slant of a hand, the family resemblance of a person.

A lowercase delta.

Her delta. Drawn the way only she drew it.

The threat that told me to stop looking for her — the threat that promised to hurt the children if I didn't walk away — was signed in her own hand.

I sat in the dark, soaked through, the fire dead around me, and the floor tilted one last time and would not come back level. Because there were only two ways that mark got onto that message, and I could not breathe past either one.

Either she had sent it herself — Clara, reaching out of the dark, warning me, telling me with the only sign she trusted that this was real, that the danger was real, that for the love of God I should stop.

Or they had found out what her sign meant.

Which meant they had her hands.

Chapter 10: Roots

There is a question you learn never to ask, in the life I used to live, because the answer is always worse than the not-knowing.

Where did it start?

Everything that hurts you has a beginning. A first cell. A first lie told to a first person who believed it. We like to think evil arrives all at once, fully grown, a monster stepping out of the dark. It doesn't. It germinates. Somebody, somewhere, on some ordinary forgettable afternoon, plants a seed in good soil and walks away, and twenty years later children are loaded into trucks in the dark and nobody remembers the afternoon.

I had spent the night before believing the worst thing I'd ever see was the threat that came signed with Clara's mark — that small lowercase sign she put at the end of everything, the one that meant me, only me, you can trust this. It had come attached to a knife aimed at my throat, and it left me sitting in the grey Brussels light with two unbearable thoughts taking turns. Either she had sent it. Or they had her hands.

I didn't sleep on it. You can't sleep on a thing like that. You can only outrun it, and the only direction I knew how to run was down — down into the work, down into the roots, down to the afternoon where it all began.

So that's what I told the thing I'd built. Forget the now. Forget the threat. Go back. Go all the way back. Show me the first seed.

Let me tell you how it feels to send it digging, because I won't insult you with how it does it.

It feels like watching someone you love walk into a forest you're afraid of.

You stand at the edge. You can't follow. You've handed it the thread and now it's gone in, between the trees, into the years, and all you can do is hold the loose end and wait, and pray it doesn't come back with something you can't unsee.

I made coffee. I let it go cold. That had become a kind of ritual, a small offering — a cup of coffee I'd never drink, sitting on the sill, going to skin, while the thing I'd made walked backward through time looking for where the rot took root.

I had taught it, the night before, to recognise a hand. The careful, scarless, surgical way the whole rotten apparatus hid itself — the Surgeon's hand, my buried brother's hand, with my own fingerprints somewhere under it. Now I sent it hunting for that hand not across the map but across the calendar. Not where but when. I wanted to know how old this thing was. I wanted to know when the first invisible door was cut.

I expected weeks. Maybe months. The recent past, the last year or two, the convoys that were running now.

It came back with years.

I want to describe what it showed me, but the truth is it didn't show me a thing — it showed me a growth. The way you'd see a tree if you could watch it for a decade in a single minute. A slow, patient, beautiful, monstrous thing, reaching.

It started small. That's what undid me, sitting there in the dark — how small it started. One quiet arrangement, years ago, in a corner of the world nobody watches. A single charity that did real good, mostly, and a little harm on the side, the way a man might skim from a till he's also keeping honest books for. One route. One truck. One man who learned he could move something that wasn't blankets inside a thing that everyone waved through because it had the right word painted on the side. Mercy. They always paint the right word on the side.

And then it grew. The system walked me through it season by season, and I watched the single quiet arrangement put down roots. Watched it find other charities, other routes, other men who learned the same lesson. Watched the careful scarless hand come in — his hand — and teach the whole thing how to hide. How to make the dirty money breathe in clean. How to make a convoy of children look, on every piece of paper that mattered, exactly like a convoy of mercy.

Years. Not a network that appeared. A network that was raised. Somebody had been at this patiently, lovingly, the way you'd raise a child or a vineyard or a long con, for longer than I'd been clean.

And the deeper it dug, the more I understood that PHANTOM MERCY — the thing that had swallowed Clara — wasn't the beginning of anything. It was a branch. A grown limb on a tree whose trunk went down further than the system could yet see, into soil I couldn't yet name. There was something older here. Something the convoys were only the visible fruit of. The thing I was fighting was not the body. It was an arm. And the arm had been growing toward Clara for years before either of us knew her name.

I sat with that the way you sit with a diagnosis.

And here is the part I'm ashamed of, so I'll say it fast, the way you tear off a bandage. A piece of me — a small, ugly, exhausted piece — was almost relieved. Because if the thing was old, if it had been growing for years, then maybe Clara hadn't walked into it by accident. Maybe she'd seen the whole tree before any of us. Maybe the woman I loved hadn't been swallowed by something random and stupid and cruel, but had stepped, eyes open, into a thing she understood better than its own makers did, the way she understood everything, three moves ahead, someone has to protect the children. That's the version where she's not a victim. That's the version where she's the smartest person in the dark. I clung to it for about a minute, because the alternative — that she'd been caught the way a child is caught, by something patient and enormous and waiting — was more than I could hold and keep working.

I made myself let go of both. Hope is a luxury. Proof is the job. I went back to the roots.

Basti called at some hour without a number. I'd stopped knowing what time it was. I knew it was night again, or still, because the window was black and the radiator had started its slow ticking, the building's heart settling for the dark.

"You're not sleeping," he said. Not a question. Basti has known me long enough to read me through a phone, through a wall, through two countries.

"Tell me something true," I said. "Something that has nothing to do with this."

There was a pause, and the soft sound of him moving, somewhere in Marseille, in the workshop that smells of brass and oil and the sea three streets away. Basti is a locksmith now. He used to be other things. He has a daughter he didn't meet until she was nine, and a way of saying her name that makes you want to be a better man.

"The mistral came in today," he said. "Cleaned the whole sky. You could see the islands. Lou wanted ice cream and I bought her ice cream and she got it on my one good jacket and I didn't even care." A breath. "That true enough for you?"

It was. It was so true it nearly broke me, and that was exactly why I'd asked for it. Because I needed to remember what I was digging toward, on the far side of all this rot — a world where a man buys his daughter ice cream and ruins his jacket and the sky is clean. A world I was trying to drag Clara back into.

"Why are you really calling," I said.

"Because Yuri called me," Basti said, and his voice changed, went careful, went quiet, and I felt the night change with it. "He says there's chatter. About you. Not your name — they don't have your name. But about the thing you've made. They know somebody built something. Somebody who can see. And they're frightened of it, Delta. The kind of frightened that makes people do the careless thing."

"Good," I said. "Let them be frightened."

"No," Basti said. "Not good. Frightened people who've spent years building something don't run. They defend it. And the thing you're poking — it's old, isn't it. I can hear it in your voice. You found out it's old."

I didn't answer. With Basti you don't have to.

"Be careful in the roots, brother," he said softly. "That's where the deepest ones live."

He didn't hang up right away. There was a silence with weight in it, the kind that means a man is deciding whether to say the next thing.

"Yuri said one more word," Basti told me finally. "He didn't want me to pass it on. He said you'd take it the wrong way." A breath. "The chatter — the people who are frightened of your machine — some of them aren't talking about it like a tool. They're talking about it like a prize. Like a thing they want to own. There are people out there, Delta, who'd let the whole rotten convoy roll on if they could get their hands on the thing that sees. You understand what I'm telling you. You didn't just build a way to find her. You built something the whole dark wants now."

"I know," I said, and I did, the way you know a draught before you find the open window.

"No, you don't," Basti said gently. "You're inside it. You can't see the size of it. I can. From out here, brother, it looks like you lit a fire to find one woman and the fire's so bright that everyone who lives in the dark just turned to look at it." A pause. "Find her fast. Before they decide they want the fire more than they fear it."

The line went dead before I could answer, which was how I knew he meant it.

After he hung up I went back in, because Basti was right and that was exactly why I couldn't stop.

Old things are the most dangerous things. A new criminal makes new mistakes. An old one has had years to bury his — to grow over the seam, to let the soil settle, to make the first afternoon look like nothing, like weather, like the way things have always been. The further back I went, the harder it was to tell the rot from the ground it grew in. That's the genius of patience. Wait long enough and your crime becomes the landscape.

But the system didn't get tired and it didn't get fooled by the landscape. I'd built it to believe only proof, and proof doesn't soften with age. A thing is true or it isn't, whether it happened last night or a lifetime ago. So it kept walking backward, sealing each step with that small unbreakable seal Clara had taught me to demand, refusing the easy story, refusing the convenient forgetting, dragging the buried afternoons one by one back up into the light.

And somewhere down in those years — past the convoys, past the laundered mercy, past even the Surgeon's first scarless cut — it found the trunk. The first arrangement. The seed. The actual beginning, with a date attached to it the way a tombstone has a date, the way a birth certificate has a date, because beginnings are both at once and I have never been more sure of that than I was right then.

The first thread. The first quiet afternoon when somebody, somewhere, decided that mercy was a good place to hide a knife.

And I saw, in that first arrangement, the shape of the thing I'd have to chase next. Because the seed wasn't a man and it wasn't a place. It was a route. A single line drawn across a map between a place that had too much and a place that had too little, with all the world's permission to carry things along it without anyone looking inside the truck. That was the trunk. That was the oldest true thing — not money, not a name, but a road that the world had agreed not to question. Everything else had grown along it like ivy along a wall. If I wanted the body the limbs belonged to, I wouldn't find it by chasing men. I'd find it by riding the rails the mercy ran on, all the way to wherever they were carrying her now. I filed that for the morning. The roads would still be there at dawn, and so, I prayed, would she.

I want to tell you I felt triumph. The hunter's triumph, the end of the trail, the root in my hand at last. For one half of one second I did. I had it. The beginning of the thing that took her. The oldest true thing in the whole rotten tree.

Then the system did the small honest thing it always does. It showed me the date.

I have a confession to make about dates.

Most of them mean nothing. They're filing. They're the grey scaffolding the world hangs itself on so the post arrives and the bills get paid. You forget them the second you read them. A man could live his whole life and remember six dates with his body instead of his head — the ones that are carved into him, the ones his heart knows even when his mind's asleep.

I have a few of those. The day I walked out of the old life. The day my mother stopped recognising my voice. And one more. One I have never said out loud to another living soul, because it was the only entirely good thing that ever happened to me and I was afraid that saying it would let the world in to spoil it.

A bar in Las Vegas that smelled of spilled bourbon and possibility. A woman who told a stranger to build her a thing that only believes proof. The night the whole scattered, restless, lost mess of me went still for the first time in my life, because someone real had walked into it and switched off the noise. The month I fell in love with Clara Dubois — completely, totally, the way I fall, the way that has always destroyed me, except this time it didn't, this time it was the one true thing.

I know that month the way I know my own pulse. I would know it on my deathbed, at the bottom of the sea, in handwriting, in the dark.

I leaned in toward the screen, toward the date the system had carried up out of all those buried years, the date the first seed of the thing that took her went into the ground. And the cold came back, the particular cold that has nothing to do with weather, the cold of a thing you already half knew and prayed against.

The trail didn't start the year she vanished. It didn't start a year before, or two.

The trail was laid the same month he and I first fell in love.

Chapter 11: The Rails

There is a lie so beautiful that no one wants to catch it.

The lie is a truck. A white truck, clean as a hospital, with a blue sticker on the side — two hands cupped around a small bird, or a flame, or a child, depending on which charity painted it — rolling down a road that other trucks are afraid to take. It carries rice. It carries blankets and powdered milk and the kind of cheap, sturdy shoes that get handed out in places where the school burned down. Children run after it. Grandmothers bless it. Soldiers wave it through the checkpoint and feel, for one minute, like the good guys.

Nobody stops a charity truck. That's the whole point. That's the genius of it, and I hated whoever thought of it the way you can only hate someone you understand.

I had spent the night before learning that the trail of Phantom Mercy was three years old — that it had been laid the same month Clara and I first fell into each other, in a desert city, a lifetime and a hand of cards ago. That had nearly broken me. So this morning, when I sat back down at the machine, I did it the way you go back to a wound: slowly, and looking somewhere else.

I asked it one question.

Show me where the trucks go.

Here is the thing I want you to feel, and I'll say it once and then never bore you with the how, because the how doesn't matter and the what will keep you up at night the way it kept me.

The world is full of routes nobody draws on a map.

The official routes — the ones the charity puts in its glossy report, the proud arrows from a warehouse in one country to a refugee camp in another — those are for the donors. Those are the routes you photograph. There is a smiling logistics manager in every one of those photographs, and he is always, always wearing a vest with a lot of pockets.

But under the routes you're allowed to see, there are routes you aren't. The truck doesn't go straight. It can't. A straight line is honest, and honesty is the one thing this cargo couldn't survive. So the truck wanders. It stops where it has no reason to stop. It waits in a yard behind a yard. It crosses a border at the hour the tired guard changes shift. It loses a few hours it can never quite account for, the way a drunk loses a Tuesday.

To anyone watching, that's just bad logistics. Trucks are late. Roads are bad. Wars move things around. Shrug.

The thing I built doesn't shrug.

It took every honest route and laid it over every real one — the real one being where the truck actually went, gathered from a thousand small breadcrumbs the world drops without meaning to — and it showed me the difference between the two. The gap. The lie in the shape of a detour.

And the gaps weren't random.

That was the moment my coffee went cold in my hand. Because random would have been innocent. Random would have been potholes and checkpoints and a driver who liked a particular roadside café. What I was looking at wasn't random. It was a system. The same detours, over and over, year after year, like a path worn into a carpet by a man pacing the same six steps while he waits for terrible news.

Six steps.

I counted them before I understood what I was counting. Six places where the honest route and the real route peeled apart and the truck went dark for a handful of hours and came back lighter — or heavier — than the manifest said it should be. Six stops that appeared in convoy after convoy, route after route, charity after charity, like a watermark you only see when you tilt the page to the light.

Six relay points.

I knew that number. Clara had given me that number. It had been sitting in her last message like a stone in a shoe, and I'd read past it a hundred times because I didn't yet have the map to put it on. Six relay points. She hadn't been guessing. She'd already walked this road in her head, alone, in the dark, with no one to call. She'd seen the worn path in the carpet and she'd counted the steps, and then she'd done the thing only Clara would do.

She'd decided to be cargo.

I had to get up and walk around the apartment. I want to be honest about that, because a confession that pretends the man telling it stayed calm is a coward's confession. I got up and I walked the four walls of that grey Brussels room like the truck walked its route — pacing the same six steps, waiting for terrible news — and I said her name out loud twice, which I'd been trying not to do, because saying it out loud made the room emptier.

Then I made myself sit down, because she would have. Clara had a gift I never had: she could be terrified and useful at the same time. I could only ever pick one. So I borrowed her, the way you borrow a steadier friend's voice in your head, and I sat back down, and I asked the machine the second question. The one that turns a horror into a case.

Show me the money.

Let me tell you about the money, in plain words, because the money is the cruelest part and the part the donors would least believe.

People give. That's the thing you have to hold on to. Ordinary people, good people, people who saw a photograph of a child with enormous eyes and felt something move in them and reached for their wallet — they give. They gave to Phantom Mercy by the hundred thousand. Small gifts, mostly. The price of a coffee. The price of a cinema ticket they decided to skip because somewhere a child needed it more. I have looked at the river of those gifts and I have wanted to lie down on the floor, because every single one of them was an act of love, and every single one of them was being used to oil the machine that was eating the children they thought they were saving.

That's the trick, you see. The convoys move the children. The donations move the guilt — they wash it. Dirty money goes in pretending to be a gift, gets stirred into the great warm river of real gifts from real kind people, and comes out the other side clean, blessed, untouchable, wearing the same blue sticker as the rice. Nobody audits a charity that's saving children. That would be obscene. That would be the act of a monster. So the monsters hid exactly there, behind the one door no decent person will kick down.

I sat in the dark and I watched the river of gifts and the river of lies run side by side and braid together until you couldn't tell which drop was which. And the thing I built — patient, tireless, with no heart to break, which is its own kind of mercy — pulled them apart. Drop by drop. It found the gifts that weren't gifts. The ones that came in too round, too clean, from too far, at the wrong hour, in a rhythm — there's that word again, the rhythm, the fingerprint, the man's hands he can never change. The same cold, careful hands I'd been chasing since I first called him the Surgeon.

His hands were on the money too.

Of course they were. He'd built the rails. The trucks were the body of the thing and the money was its blood, and he'd designed both to look like charity, and he'd done it with the patient, elegant cruelty of a man who treated children as weight and donors as fools and the whole grieving, generous world as a thing to be steered.

I felt the admiration try to come back. That sick professional warmth — look what he made, look how it breathes. And I heard Yuri in my head, the way he'd said it down the line from Kyiv with a drone going over: Admire him from the outside. The second you climb into his head to appreciate the artistry, you stop seeing the children.

So I didn't admire it. I counted it.

Two thousand, eight hundred and forty-seven.

That was the number of poisoned gifts the machine pulled out of the river. Two thousand, eight hundred and forty-seven separate little lies wearing the face of kindness, stretched across four countries, three years, and a dozen names too clean to be true. I didn't choose that number. I want to be clear. I didn't go looking for it to make the night feel literary. The machine handed it to me the way it handed me everything — flatly, without drama, a bird laid at my feet — and I sat and looked at it and felt the floor of the world tilt, because two thousand, eight hundred and forty-seven is not a number you survive looking at all at once. It's a number you have to look at one drop at a time or it drowns you.

Twelve of those names mattered more than the rest. Twelve hands that the river kept washing toward, again and again, like a tide returning the same drowned thing to the same shore. I didn't have their faces yet. I had the shape of where they touched the money, the way the night before I'd had the shape of where they'd been. Twelve. I wrote it down on the back of an envelope, under the six, because some numbers you don't trust to a screen. Six relays. Twelve hands. And the river, and the trucks, and Clara somewhere inside the body of the beast, holding her breath, keeping her own count.

I called Basti, because I needed to hear a human being who lived where the road ended.

Basti is in Marseille. He used to open things that weren't his, in the years when we were both younger and the rules felt optional, and then one day he started opening doors for people who'd locked themselves out instead of people who'd locked something away, and he calls that going straight and I call it going sideways with better manners, and we love each other the way only two reformed thieves can.

He answered with his mouth full. That's how I knew he was alright. The day Basti answers and isn't eating is the day I'll start praying.

"It's the middle of the night where you are," he said. "Or it's the middle of the night where you live, anyway. You don't have a where anymore, do you, you sad orphan."

"Basti." I closed my eyes. "The aid convoys. The white trucks. The charity ones, the ones nobody stops."

A pause. A swallow. When he spoke again the joking had gone out of his voice the way the tide goes out, all at once, leaving the flat hard sand. "What about them."

"They come through Marseille."

"Everything comes through Marseille," he said. "That's what a port is. It's the world's back door, and I grew up holding it open." Another pause. I could hear his city behind him — a scooter, a gull, someone laughing two floors down, the whole warm noisy life of the place. "Why are you asking me about the white trucks, Delta. Ask me something else."

"Because they're not carrying rice."

He didn't say anything for a long time. Long enough that I thought the line had dropped, the way Clara's line had dropped, the way they all dropped now in my imagination, one by one, everyone I loved going dark mid-sentence.

"Basti."

"I'm here." His voice had gone quiet and strange. "I'm here. I just — listen. There's a yard. Behind the old fish market, where they don't sell fish anymore, where nobody sells anything anymore. I've watched the white trucks go in there at hours when no charity in the world is working. I told myself it was nothing. I told myself, Basti, you have a guilty mind, you see crime everywhere because you used to be it." He took a breath I could hear. "Tell me I was wrong to leave it alone. Tell me, and I'll go and stand in that yard tonight."

"No," I said, and it came out harder than I meant, because the thought of Basti standing in that yard, in the dark, alone, while the Surgeon's cold hands moved somewhere over a map — it put a cold of its own straight through me. "No. You don't go near it. Not yet. Not until I know more. Promise me."

"You've never told me not to open a door before."

"I'm telling you now."

He laughed, but it was thin. "Now he learns caution. The man who taught me to be reckless. She really did change you, your Clara." And then, gently, because under all the food and the filthy jokes Basti is the kindest man I know: "We'll get her out, brother. The trucks always have to come back empty. And an empty truck is a confession. You taught me that."

He hung up first. They always hang up first. I sat with the dead line and the cold coffee and the envelope with the two numbers on it, and outside the Brussels morning went on buttering its bread.

I went back to the trucks. I had to.

Because there was one more thing the machine was still chewing on, quietly, in the corner where it kept the parts of the picture I hadn't asked to see yet. The routes had a future. That's the thing about a worn path in a carpet — it doesn't just tell you where a man has paced. It tells you he's going to pace it again. The convoys ran on a schedule. Cruelty, it turns out, keeps office hours. There had been one last month. One the month before that. One every few weeks, regular as a heartbeat, regular as the rhythm of those poisoned gifts, the Surgeon's cold hands keeping perfect time.

Which meant there was a next one.

I asked. I didn't want to. There are questions you ask the way you reach into the dark under a bed where you've heard something move. But Clara was inside the body of the beast, holding her breath, keeping her count, and if the beast was about to move, then she was about to move with it, and I needed to know how long I had.

The machine answered the way it always did. Flatly. A bird at my feet.

It put two things on the screen and I read them both, and then I read them again, the way I'd read the Surgeon's name was mine, the way the truth always makes me read it twice because the first time it just sits on the surface and refuses to be true.

The next convoy left in forty hours.

And the manifest counted its cargo as "47 units."

Chapter 12: War-Gaming

Forty hours.

That was the number now. Somewhere out on the iron veins of the country, a long train was being loaded in the dark, and on the paper that travelled with it, the children had a column and a unit and a count, and the count was forty-seven, and the train left in forty hours.

I had spent my whole life racing clocks. I had never once hated one the way I hated that one.

You'd think a number like that would make you move faster. It does the opposite. It freezes you. You sit very still in the centre of your own panic, because every direction looks like the wrong one, and you understand — finally, completely — that if you pick wrong, real children pay for it with the only lives they will ever get. That kind of fear doesn't speed you up. It makes you careful in a way that feels like drowning.

So I did the most frightening thing I know how to do.

I stopped chasing the man across the river.

And I sat down to become him.

Here's the thing nobody tells you about hunting someone clever. You will never catch him by being faster. He's had years. He's three moves ahead before you've found the board. If you sprint after him you'll arrive, breathless, at exactly the place he wanted you, every single time, and you'll call it progress because you're tired and tired men confuse motion with meaning.

You catch a man like that one way only. You stop asking where is he and start asking what would I do, if I were him, and I were good, and I had no soul left to slow me down.

You play him. You sit on his side of the table. You pick up his pieces. And you try, with everything you have, to beat yourself.

I'd done it a hundred times in the old life, for money, for clients who paid me to think like their enemy so they could sleep at night. It was the one honest skill I had — the ability to climb inside another person's want and wear it like a coat. It's also, if you're wondering, exactly the same skill that ruined every love I ever had. The man who can become anyone has a terrible time staying himself. Clara knew that about me before I knew it about myself. You disappear into people, she'd said once, not unkindly, tracing a line on my chest in the dark. It's why you're so good at finding them. And it's why I'll always be a little afraid you'll find someone and forget to come back.

I hadn't forgotten to come back. Not from her. She was the first one I never wanted to disappear into and lose — I wanted to disappear into her and stay.

Forty hours.

I opened the windows. The Brussels cold came in like a slap, which is what I wanted. Then I built the war room.

I won't tell you how the tool does what it does next. I promised myself I'd never explain the machinery of it, partly because I can't do it without sounding like a man reading an instruction manual at a funeral, and partly because the how would steal the only thing that matters, which is what it gave me.

So let me just tell you what it gave me.

It let me rehearse him.

That's the closest I can get. I fed it the Surgeon — everything I'd learned of his hands, his patience, his contempt for noise, his cold elegant housekeeping cruelty — and then I asked it to do a thing that still, when I think about it, raises the hair on my arms. I asked it to play him. To take everything the man had ever done and use it to guess what he'd do next. To run the next move before he made it. To rehearse the enemy's morning, so that when it came, I'd already have lived it.

It's like this. Imagine you could sit down across a chessboard from your worst enemy — except the version of him sitting there is built from his own habits, his own appetites, his own thousand past games, so accurate it makes your skin crawl. And you can play him. Over and over. Lose to him. Watch how he wins. Reset the board and play again. Until you've felt every blade he carries and you know — not guess, know — the shape of the cut he'll choose.

That's what the war room was. A place where I could lose to the Surgeon a thousand times before breakfast, so that I'd only have to win once, when it counted, in the real world, where you don't get to reset the board and the children don't come back.

I started playing.

Basti called in the middle of it.

Basti is Marseille. Basti is a man who used to open other people's locks for a living and now opens his own bar, which is to say he found a legal way to do the one thing his hands were made for, which is more than most of us manage. He's loud, he's filthy, he loves me in the way that men who've been to prison love the man who didn't let them go alone. He calls when he can feel I'm in trouble. He always can. It's annoying.

"You're doing the thing," he said.

"What thing."

"The thing where you become the bad guy and forget to eat and forget to call your friends and forget you have a face we'd all like to see at a table some time before we die." A glass clinked. Morning, and Basti was already polishing something. "Yuri called me. He's worried. Yuri being worried is like the Pope being worried. It travels."

"I'm fine, Basti."

"You're not fine, you're useful," he said, "which with you is a different and more dangerous thing." He sighed, and under the noise I heard the harbour, gulls, a city that smelled like salt and diesel and the south, a city Clara's last paper had pointed at like an accusing finger, a city I'd sworn I'd never set foot in again and was going to have to. "Listen to me, you beautiful idiot. When you do this — when you climb into a man — you bring something back on your shoes. You always do. The last time you war-gamed somebody for three days straight, you came out of it and you couldn't look at your own hands for a week. Remember?"

I remembered.

"So play him," Basti said. "Play him to win. But set an alarm, eh? Set an alarm to remind yourself which one of you is you. Because the kind of man you're playing — the kind that does this to children — you go too deep into a head like that, you might not feel the door close behind you."

There it was again. The door. The same door Clara had warned me about in a desert bar three years before. The same door Yuri had named the night I found the Surgeon's fingerprint in my own old work. My whole family, scattered across a dozen broken cities, all of them watching the same door, all of them terrified I'd be the one who finally walked through it and didn't come back.

"I'll set an alarm," I said.

"Liar," Basti said, with love, and hung up — because that's what they do, my family, they deliver the love and hang up before they have to hear what it costs me to receive it.

I didn't set an alarm.

I went back into the Surgeon's head.

It is a strange thing to spend a morning losing on purpose.

I let him beat me. Again and again. I'd lay out a move — what if I were him, what would I reach for next — and the rehearsal would unfold it, cold and fast, and show me where it led, and almost every time it led somewhere that made my stomach turn, because the Surgeon's mind ran on a logic that had no brakes. He didn't ask is this allowed. He didn't ask who gets hurt. He asked only what is the cleanest cut, and then he made it, and the cleanest cut was always, always through the softest thing in the room.

The first few times I tried to play him like I'd play a clever man. Pressure here. Misdirection there. Protect what matters, sacrifice what doesn't.

I kept losing because I kept making the mistake of having a heart.

He didn't protect what mattered. He had nothing that mattered. That was the whole horror of him and it was also, God help me, his strength — you cannot threaten a man who loves nothing. You cannot get behind him by going for the thing he cares about, because there is no thing. Every move I made that assumed he'd flinch to save someone, he simply didn't make that flinch, and so he was already past me, already through the seam, already standing in the room I'd been trying to keep him out of, with that quiet stillness, that housekeeping.

So I made myself a smaller, colder man. I cut my own heart out and set it on the desk beside the cold tea and I played again.

And I started to win some.

That should have felt good. It felt like dying a little. Because the version of me that could match him — that could think his clean, brakeless, soulless way — that man had once been real. I'd retired him. Clara had helped me bury him. And here I was, on a grey Brussels morning with forty hours on the clock, digging him back up because he was the only weapon sharp enough for the work. You build now, Clara had said, like a sentence I had to live up to. But to find her I was going to have to put the breaker's clothes back on, one more time, and pray I could take them off again after.

I won a round. Then another. I started to feel the rhythm of him — the patience, the contempt for noise, the way he never reached for the obvious throat. I started, with a sick fluency, to anticipate him.

And then the rehearsal turned, the way a tide turns, and showed me what he'd do next.

It came up slow. That's how the worst ones always come — not a bang, but a developing photograph, the grey water remembering it was a face. I'd asked the simplest question in the world. He knows I'm hunting him now. He felt me find his fingerprint. He's good, and frightened men aren't this good — so he isn't frightened, he's hungry. He's a craftsman who just learned another craftsman is in his cathedral. What does a man like that do next?

He doesn't run. Running is noise.

He doesn't hide. Hiding is admitting you're prey.

He does the thing I would have done. The thing the old me, the buried me, the man I'd just dug up and put back on, would absolutely do.

He stops hunting the mission.

And he starts hunting the hunter.

I want to slow down here, because this is the part where the floor went out from under me, and I owe it to you to make you feel the floor go.

For days I'd been thinking of myself as the spider. I had the web — the family, the system, the great patient machine I'd built in seven sleepless nights to find the woman I loved. I sat in the middle of it and I felt the threads tremble and I told myself I was closing on the dark.

But there are two animals at a web. There's the spider. And there's the thing that learns the spider lives there, and walks up the silk, slow, on legs that don't tremble it, all the way to the centre, to where the spider sits, fat and certain, feeling clever.

I'd been so busy reading the Surgeon that I'd forgotten the most basic, most obvious, most me thing in the world: a man like that, the moment he knows you exist, does not wait to be found. He finds out who you are. He finds out what you love. He finds out where you sleep. Because that's what I used to do. That's how I used to win. You don't beat the other genius at his own game. You go around the board entirely and you put your hand on the thing he can't bear to lose, and you let him beat himself trying to save it.

The rehearsal ran it out for me, cold and clean and certain, the way it ran everything. It took the Surgeon and it asked: knowing what he knows, wanting what he wants, with no heart to slow him — where does he strike next?

Not the case. Not the train. Not the children.

Me.

He'd come for me. To stop the one hunter who could see his hands. And the rehearsal — patient, bloodless, proud of itself, laying the bird at my feet — narrowed it down the way it always did, from the whole formless dark to a single point, the way it had once narrowed a whole country to one street.

It narrowed his next move to an address.

And the address was familiar. The address was mine — not where I was, that was a secret even my family half-guessed at, but the one place in the world that still had my real name on a piece of paper, the place I'd grown up, the place I went back to only in dreams and never told a living soul about. The system had it because the system had everything. The Surgeon had it because the Surgeon was me, the cold me, the buried me, and the buried me knew exactly how to find a man by finding the one door he never thought anyone could.

I stared at it. My heart was going like I'd run up six flights. The rehearsal didn't ask me to like it. The rehearsal didn't ask me anything. It had played the enemy against me a thousand times that morning, and the enemy had, every time he chose to come for the hunter, walked the silk to the same centre, put his hand on the same soft thing, made the same clean cut.

The rehearsal predicted the next target with sickening certainty: his own front door.

Chapter 13: Breadcrumbs

The thing told me my own front door was next, and then it sat there waiting for me to do something human about it.

I did the most human thing I know. I made coffee, burned my hand on the pot, swore in three languages at a radiator that had no opinion on the matter, and stood at the window of the Brussels apartment watching the rain come down on a street where, somewhere, a man might already be deciding the best hour to come for me.

You'd think a warning like that would make a man run. It didn't. It made me still. The same stillness as always — the old, cold, careful stillness I'd worn like a second skin in the life I'd buried, the one that used to come on right before I went somewhere I had no business going. Except I don't go places like that anymore. I find people. I bring them home.

And I had just understood something, standing there with my scalded hand around a cup I couldn't taste.

If they were coming for me, it meant the hunt was working. You don't kick down a man's door to make him stop unless he's getting close. The threat signed in Clara's own hand, the fire the Surgeon had set, and now this — the cold arithmetic that said his front door is next — they weren't the network being strong. They were the network being scared. I'd rattled something. The fact that they wanted me gone meant I was finally somewhere worth being.

So I didn't run. I sat back down in front of the thing I'd built, the thing a machine had helped me make in seven sleepless days for exactly this, and I asked it to do the one job it does better than any creature that breathes.

I asked it to pick up the trail.
