I want to be precise about that minute, because it's the kind of thing a man doesn't admit later, when he's telling the story over wine to people who want him to have been brave the whole way through. I was not brave the whole way through. When the system laid that last truth at my feet — the one door is rotten, the one clean man is dirty, there is no door — I sat in the dark of the Brussels apartment with my hands flat on the desk and I felt the whole beautiful structure I'd built all night sway like a tower in a wind. A night of sealing the truth thread by thread, of doing it the slow holy way Clara would have demanded, and the only person on earth allowed to carry that truth into the light was a man who took their money to keep it buried.

I'd built a perfect key and there was no lock that would have it.

And under the panic, lower down, in the old basement of me where the wild man still lives and never fully sleeps, a voice said, very reasonably: So burn him too. Burn them all. You don't need a prosecutor to set a fire.

I didn't answer it. I'd learned by then that you don't argue with that voice, you just don't feed it, the way you don't argue with a dog that's decided it loves you — you simply walk to a different room. So I stood up. I walked to the window. Marseille was eleven hundred kilometres south of me, somewhere past the curve of the dark, and Clara was in it, spending herself, drop by drop, and I had spent the whole night building a weapon I couldn't fire and now the sun was coming up grey over a city full of people who got to sleep through this.

I was so far inside my own despair that I almost didn't hear the system change its breathing.

That's the only way I know how to say it. The tool I'd built had a kind of breathing, a steady background hum of all the small things it watched for me, and over six days I'd learned its rhythm so deeply that I felt it in my body the way you feel a familiar car's engine, the way you'd know in your sleep if the person beside you stopped breathing. And somewhere behind me, while I stood at the window hating the dawn, the rhythm changed.

It found something.

It found something it had been listening for, in the dark, for six days, without ever once telling me, because I hadn't asked it to hope and so it hadn't.

I turned around very slowly, the way you turn when you've heard a sound in an empty house and you're not ready yet to know what it is.

Let me tell you what I'd done, days before, that I'd half forgotten.

Early in the week — back when I still had hope to spare, back before the marker games and the rotten prosecutor and the slow grinding loss of every clean idea — I had asked the system to do something that, even as I asked it, felt like a prayer dressed up as a task. I had given it everything that was hers. Not her face. Not her phone. Those were gone, scrubbed, dark. I gave it the smaller things. The shape of how she moved through the world. The particular signature of a particular brilliant careful woman — the way she'd leave a trace without leaving a trace, the habits she couldn't unlearn even with a gun at her back, the tiny tells of a mind that had spent its whole life proving things.

And I'd said to it, in plain words, alone, feeling like a fool: If she's alive — if she's anywhere — listen for her. Not for where she is. For whether she is. Listen to the silence and tell me if her heart is still in it.

I'd asked for proof of life. Not a location. Not a rescue. Just the one thing, the only thing, the thing under all the other things. Is she still breathing.

And then I'd let it go, because you can't sit and watch a thing like that, you'd go mad, and I'd buried myself in the routes and the money and the twelve faces and the rotten door, and somewhere underneath all of it the system had kept listening. Patiently. Without hope, because it doesn't have any. Without despair, for the same reason. Just listening to the silence where Clara was supposed to be, the way you'd hold a stethoscope to a chest and wait, and wait, and wait.

For six days the silence had stayed silent.

And now, at dawn, with the worst news of my life still warm at my feet, it had heard something move in the dark.

I crossed the room so fast I knocked the chair over.

I'm not going to pretend I read it calmly. I fell into the seat like a man falling into water and I stared at what the system was showing me and for a second I genuinely could not make my eyes resolve it, the way you can't read a word you've been waiting your whole life to read.

It wasn't a map. It wasn't a name. It wasn't a face.

It was a line.

A single line, moving across the dark of the screen — small, faint, fragile as a thread of smoke — rising and falling, rising and falling, in a rhythm I would have known at the bottom of the sea. The system had taken the faintest signal in the world, a thing so quiet that for six days even it couldn't be sure it was real, and it had pulled that signal up out of the noise and the static and the lies, cleaned it, steadied it, and laid it in front of me as a line on a screen that rose and fell.

It took me a moment to understand what I was looking at.

It was a heartbeat.

It was her heartbeat.

It was Clara's heart, beating, somewhere out there in the cruel dark, and the thing I'd built had reached into all that distance and found it and was holding it up to me like a man cupping a candle flame in two hands against the wind, saying, in the only language it had: Here. She's here. She's alive. Look — she's alive.

I made a sound I'm not proud of and will not describe.

You have to understand what I had been carrying.

For six days I'd been doing all of it — the building, the hunting, the sealing of truth, the war with my own worst self — on top of a hole. Underneath every clever thing I did all week there had been a pit with no bottom, and the name of the pit was what if she's already gone. What if I was building a monument. What if the whole frantic beautiful machine, the platform I'd torn out of myself in seven sleepless days, the case, the redemption, the slow holy right-way plan, all of it was a thing I was constructing over a grave. What if Clara had been dead since the second night and I was just a man having a very elaborate conversation with a ghost.

I had not let myself say that out loud. Not to Ifeoma, not to Yuri, not even to the dark at three in the morning. Because to say it was to make it possible, and I could not make it possible, I would not, I needed her alive the way a drowning man needs the next inch of air, and so I had simply refused — had stacked the refusal up day after day until it was a wall — and built and built and built on top of it and never once looked down.

And now there was a line on a screen rising and falling, rising and falling, and it meant the pit was empty. There was no grave. There was a woman, somewhere, with a heart in her chest, doing the bravest thing a heart can do, which is to keep going when everything around it is built to make it stop.

I put my hand flat against the screen like an idiot, like a man who's never seen the thing he made.

I just wanted to be near it.

Basti called.

I'd forgotten the phone was even in my hand. Basti — my locksmith, my fixer, the smuggler turned good who handles the boots and the doors and the parts of this world you can touch — Basti was already in Marseille, already in position, already doing the patient ugly groundwork while I sat in Brussels and made the truth heavy. He has a voice like gravel in a tin and a heart he hides behind it badly.

"You sound strange," he said, before I'd said anything. "You been crying? Tell me you been crying. I'll feel better about my whole week if the genius cried."

"She's alive," I said. My voice came out wrong, thin, a boy's voice. "Basti. She's alive. I'm — I can see it. I can see her heart."

A silence on the line. Then a long, slow exhale, the sound of a big man sitting down on something.

"You're sure." Not a question, the way Basti asks questions. "Not a guess. Not a hope. You don't get to do hope to me, Delta, I got no defences against your hope, you do hope to me and I will drive into that building tonight whether you say go or not."

"It's not hope." I laughed, and it cracked in the middle. "That's the thing, Basti. It's not hope. Hope's the thing I've been fighting all week. This is the opposite of hope. This is — it's the thing under hope. It's real. The system isn't telling me she might be alive. It's telling me she is. Right now. As we speak. Her heart is beating and I'm watching it."

I heard him swallow. Basti, who cries at dog films and pretends it's allergies, who carried a man's coffin in Marseille two years ago and didn't make a sound, and who now, on a phone, eleven hundred kilometres away, made one small sound and covered it with a cough.

"Okay," he said roughly. "Okay. Then she's waiting for us. Then we don't have a body to find, we got a person, and a person can be brought home." A pause. "You tell Ifeoma yet?"

"You're the first."

"Good." I could hear the grin trying to climb back into his voice and failing. "Then I get to be the one who knew before Lagos. She'll never forgive me. Worth it." Then, lower, all the gravel gone soft: "Bring her home, brother. We got a heartbeat. That's more than we had an hour ago. That's everything."

He hung up. My family hangs up before you can thank them. I've told you that. It's a rule none of us ever agreed to and all of us keep.

I sat with it.

I let myself sit with it, just for a minute, just to be a man watching the woman he loves be alive, before I had to become the other thing again, the thing that plans, the thing that fights, the thing that solves the small impossible problem of a rotten door and a dying clock. I let the wall I'd built all week — the wall over the pit — I let it come down, gently, brick by brick, because there was no pit under it after all, there was a heartbeat, and you can let a wall down when there's solid ground beneath.

I thought about the first time I'd ever felt that heartbeat. Las Vegas, three years ago, a hotel room with the air conditioning roaring and the city throwing its colours against the curtains, and Clara asleep against my chest with her ear over my heart, and me lying awake — me, who never lies still, who never stays, who dissolves into every feeling and forgets the world — lying perfectly, perfectly still so as not to wake her, feeling her heart through her back where it pressed against me, thinking: this. I could do this. I could finally hold still for this one. I hadn't known yet that I'd lose her. I hadn't known yet that I'd have to build a god out of code in seven days to find her again. I'd just known the rhythm of her, the small steady miracle of it, the way it ran a little fast when she was thinking hard, even in her sleep.

The line on the screen rose and fell. Rose and fell. A little fast. She was thinking hard, even now. Of course she was. She was the smartest operator in the book, and she was inside the thing, and she was still working, I could feel it, I could feel her mind running behind that brave fast heart, building her own case, drop by drop, in the dark, trusting — I knew this with a certainty that hollowed me out — trusting that I would find the genome and come.

"I found it," I said, out loud, to a screen, to a line, to a woman a thousand kilometres south who couldn't hear me. "I found your heartbeat, Clara. I found you. Hold on. I'm coming. The right way. I swear to you, the right way, even now, even like this — I'm coming, and I'm bringing the law with me, and we're going to prove them, all of them, every face — just hold on—"

And the heartbeat stopped.

I want to tell you that I stayed calm.

I didn't. The line went flat — just flat, just gone, the rise and fall I'd been living inside for the last quarter of an hour simply ceased, the way a song ceases when someone lifts the needle, and the silence that came up where her heart had been was the loudest silence I have ever heard in my life. The whole world tipped. I was on my feet without deciding to stand. I had her name in my mouth and no air to put behind it.

One second.

Two.

The right-way man, the slow holy builder, the redeemed one — he was gone. There was only the wild thing left, the thing in the basement, on its feet, screaming, ready to burn the whole earth down to its bones if her heart did not start again.

Three seconds. Four.

Start. Start. Start, Clara, start, you brave impossible woman, you do not get to do this, not now, not after six days, not when I'm watching — start—

Five. Six. Seven.

I was begging. Out loud. To a machine. To a line. To God, whom I do not believe in and was, in that moment, on my knees before anyway.

Eight.

The heartbeat stopped for nine seconds.

Then it moved — faster than any human could run.

Chapter 20: Two Fires

The heartbeat moved faster than any human could run, and for nine seconds I had let myself believe she was free.

I want to be honest about those nine seconds, because they were the cruellest mercy of the whole week. For nine seconds the silence broke and her heartbeat came back and then it ran — across the map, across the dead north of that city, faster than legs, faster than fear, faster than anything with a pulse should move. And the animal part of me, the part that had spent six days starving, leapt up and said she's out, she's running, she made it. I was already half out of my chair. I was already reaching for my coat.

Then the cold part of me — the part Clara built, the part that had kept me alive every night that the warm part wanted to get me killed — caught the warm part by the collar and held it still and made it look again.

People don't move that fast. Hope does. A heartbeat doesn't sprint across a city in nine seconds. But a marker does, if a hand is carrying it. A signal does, if someone has picked it up and is walking it somewhere to make a man like me run after it.

It wasn't Clara running.

It was Clara's heartbeat being carried.

And the moment I understood that — the moment the warm part sat back down, sick and ashamed of its own joy — I understood the other thing too, the thing I'd been refusing to look at since the marker first lied to me. The Surgeon wasn't trying to lose me anymore. He'd stopped running. He was steering me. He had her pulse in his hand like a lure on a string, and he was dragging it across the water, watching to see which way the hungry thing would turn.

So I made myself do the unbearable thing.

I let it run.

I sat there and I watched the heartbeat I'd bled for go skidding off into the dark and I did not follow it. I let the lure swing. Because the only way to beat a man who knows exactly how you think is to do, just once, the thing he's certain you can't.

I let her go, to keep her.

Then the second fire started, and the night went from impossible to something worse.

It came through Ifeoma, the way the bad things always did, because Ifeoma watches the bones of everything and the bones are where the cracks show first. She came onto the wire from Lagos and her voice was wrong — not afraid, Ifeoma is never simply afraid, but braced, the way you brace when the floor's already tilting and you're deciding which thing to grab.

"We have a problem," she said. "A real one. Right now."

"I know. He moved her marker again, he's pulling me—"

"No. Listen to me." A pause, and in the pause I heard her decide to frighten me, which Ifeoma never does unless it's earned. "Somebody is taking the whole thing apart from the inside. Not the prosecutor. Not the building. Us. The room. The thing you built. Someone has their hands inside our own walls and they are pulling out the pins one at a time, and if they finish before we move, the rescue dies in its bed and nobody ever has to fire a shot."

The floor I'd been standing on for six days quietly informed me it was not, in fact, a floor.

Let me put this in human words, because it's the whole of the chapter and I won't fog it. Everything we'd built — the case, the map, the plan, the lawful hammer waiting to fall at the lawful hour — all of it stood on one quiet thing. The thing I'd made. The system. It was the spine of all of it: it held the proof that couldn't be argued with, it held the map of the dead hospital drawn so true I could walk it blind, it held the threads of two thousand eight hundred and forty-seven movements of dirty money sealed so tight no lawyer on earth could pry them loose. Pull the spine, and the body falls. And someone — somewhere out in the dark, with patient bloodless hands I'd have known anywhere — had gotten a grip on the spine and started, very gently, to break it.

It was the Surgeon. Of course it was the Surgeon. The marker running across the map wasn't the trap. The marker was the distraction. He'd lit a fire in the front of the house to send me running for water, and while I ran he'd slipped in the back to burn the thing that mattered.

I'd taught a whole generation of frightened companies this exact trick, in the life I'm trying to leave. You don't beat a defender by being stronger. You beat him by giving him two emergencies and a single pair of hands.

Now someone was doing it to me. With the woman I loved as the kindling.

"Talk to me," I said. "How bad."

"Bad in a smart way," Ifeoma said, and I could hear her already moving, already inside it, her fingers somewhere I couldn't see, fighting. "Not loud. If it was loud I'd just slam the doors. It's quiet. It's clever. He's not smashing anything — he's untying it. Thread by thread. And here's the part you're not going to like." She stopped. The generator coughed behind her, that homely cough, absurd against the size of what she was about to say. "I can't hold all of it. There's too much and there's one of me and the clock's already running. I can save the case, or I can save the rescue. I cannot do both. The hands aren't there. The time isn't there. You built one beautiful thing and he's set it on fire in two places at once and you have to tell me, right now, which fire we let burn."

There it was.

The cruelty of it was so pure, so surgical, that for a second I almost admired it the way you admire a knife. He hadn't beaten me. He'd done something worse. He'd handed me the one choice no man should have to make and stepped back to watch which half of myself I'd kill.

The case, or the rescue.

The proof, or the door.

The thing Clara had bled her whole brave life to build — the unbreakable record, the truth heavy enough that no power on earth could lift it, the thing that meant the children stayed home and the twelve faces went somewhere they could never reach a child again — or the thing that meant Clara herself walked out of that dead building alive tonight.

You understand the trap inside the trap. If I saved the rescue, I'd get her out and the case would crumble — and a year from now, in a courtroom, twelve men would stand up and brush the truth off their shoulders like lint, and walk into the sunlight, and start again, and Clara would look at me across whatever life we'd salvaged and know, without my ever saying it, that I'd thrown away the only thing she'd ever asked the world for. And if I saved the case, I'd have my unbreakable, beautiful, sealed yes — and no door for her to come home through.

He'd built me a choice where every answer was a betrayal. That was his signature. I'd seen it once before, years ago, in a thing he made, and it had made me sick then with how elegant it was, how cold, and I'd told myself the man who built that was someone I'd never have to meet.

I was meeting him now. Through the wall, in the dark, with his hands inside my house.

"Delta." Ifeoma's voice, gentle now, because she knew what she was asking. "I need an answer. Every second you spend deciding, he wins a thread. I'm not going to choose this for you. It's not mine to choose. But I need it now."

I closed my eyes.

And here is the thing I have never told anyone, the thing I'm only telling you because this whole book is a confession and a confession that flinches is just a brag. In that second, with the woman I love being carried across a map like bait and the truth she'd died for coming apart in my friend's exhausted hands, the old man woke up in me. The one who breaks things. He woke up and he was calm and he was certain and he said, very reasonably, the most seductive sentence I have ever heard:

You don't have to choose. Burn it all down.

Stop saving. Stop building. Forget the case, forget the clean way, forget the courtroom a year from now. Go dirty. Go now. Take everything I'd sworn off and pour it onto these people like petrol and drop a match and walk into that dead hospital through the ash and carry her out over the bodies of the men who took her, and let the truth burn with everything else, because what is truth worth against her actual breathing body, against the actual warmth of her, against three years of wanting and six days of dying a little every hour. Who is evidence for. Evidence is for people who still have something left to lose.

It would have felt so good. That's the part nobody warns you about evil — that it doesn't feel like evil from the inside. It feels like relief.

And then I heard her voice. Not on the wire. In the memory, where she lives now, the hotel bar that smelled of cheap whisky, the glass she turned without looking at me. Anyone can destroy. It takes nothing. A child can knock down a tower. The hard thing — the only thing that ever actually saves anyone — is to build something so true it can't be knocked down.

She wasn't asking me to choose between her and the case.

She was asking me to be the kind of man who could find a third thing.

"Ifeoma," I said, and my voice came out steadier than I had any right to. "How long can you hold the rescue alone if you let the case go?"

"If I drop the case and put everything into the rescue?" A breath. "Long enough. Maybe. If nothing else breaks. But you'd be throwing away the—"

"I'm not throwing it away." I was already moving now, already inside it, the cold part of me running the room while the warm part screamed. "The case doesn't have to live in our house tonight. It just has to live somewhere he can't reach it before dawn. Somewhere with its own walls. Somewhere with a flag on it."

The line went very quiet.

"Marchetti," Ifeoma breathed.

"Marchetti." Because the prosecutor was bought, the one clean door was rotten, but Marchetti was something else — a hard weary man with a real flag and real walls and a vault of his own, the lawful kind, the kind I couldn't build because I'm a ghost with warrants in three countries. I couldn't keep the case safe. But I could hand it to him, sealed and whole, push the entire unbreakable truth out of my burning house and into his — and then turn, with both my hands finally free, and throw everything I had left into getting Clara out. Let the Surgeon burn an empty room. Let him pull the pins out of a house I'd already evacuated. The case would be gone — out of my reach, out of his reach, behind a flag.

It would cost me. I want to be clear about that. The moment I pushed the case into Marchetti's hands, I lost control of it. I was trusting the truth Clara bled for to a man I trusted exactly as far as the next reversal — a man who, three jurisdictions ago, I'd promised myself I'd never need. I was handing my redemption to a stranger and praying he was the man Clara thought he was.

But it was the third thing. It was the door out of a room with no doors. Save the rescue with my own hands. Save the case by giving it away. Refuse the choice the monster built for me.

"Do it," Ifeoma said, and there was something fierce in her now, something that wasn't fear at all. "Push it to Marchetti. I'll hold the rescue. Go."

I went.

It is a strange thing, to perform a rescue and a salvage in the same ten minutes, with the same shaking hands, while the heartbeat of the woman you love drags itself across a map as bait. I won't tell you how it was done. I told you a long time ago I wouldn't, and I'm not breaking that on the night it cost the most. But I'll tell you what it felt like, because the feeling is the only true thing I have to give you.

It felt like standing in a burning house with a child in one arm and a letter in the other — a letter that proves who set the fire — and knowing you can only carry one of them through the door. And finding, at the last second, a window. Pushing the letter out the window into the hands of a man on the lawn you're not even sure you trust. And then turning, with the child, toward the flames.

I pushed the case out the window.

Marchetti caught it. I felt it land — felt the whole sealed weight of it leave my house and settle behind his flag, behind his walls, where the Surgeon's patient hands couldn't follow. The truth was safe. Out of my reach, but safe. The children would still go home. The twelve faces would still fall, in daylight, forever.

And I turned, with both hands free at last, and threw everything that was left of me into the rescue. Into the dead hospital. Into the way in I'd drawn so true I could walk it blind. Into getting her out before the heartbeat-bait ran off the edge of the world and took my heart with it.

For one clean minute, it worked. It worked. I had refused the monster's choice. I had found the third thing. The case lived; the rescue lived; I was holding both fires at arm's length and not letting either one have her.

I should have known there's no such thing as a choice with no cost. He'd built it too well for that. When you save an operation by sacrificing one piece of it, you don't get to choose, in the chaos, which piece pays.

Because to push the case out the window, to free my hands for her, I'd had to let one part of the house go dark. One thread. One small piece of the whole, dropped in the rush, abandoned to the fire so the rest could live. I didn't even feel it go. You never feel the one that matters until later.

It was the eyes on the south road. The piece that watched the way out. The one I'd let burn so the rest could live.

And that was exactly where Basti was standing.

Chapter 21: The Skeleton

I had Basti somewhere I couldn't see him, and a fire I'd lit on purpose burning right where he was standing, and the only way I could think to keep from going mad about it was to learn the inside of the building that was about to swallow us both.

So that's what I did. While the night ground on and my friend stood in the dark of a city I'd run from, holding a position I'd chosen for him over his own safety, I sat in Brussels and I taught myself the bones of the place that held Clara.

Let me be honest about the shame of it first, because if I skip the shame you'll think I'm a better man than I am. Some part of me reached for the building plans the way a coward reaches for a map of the exits — so I wouldn't have to think about Basti. So I could fill my head with corridors and stairwells instead of the picture that kept trying to form, the one where I'd traded a piece of my own family to save the rest, and the piece was him.

In the last hour I'd had to make the kind of choice that no amount of cleverness ever softens. Two fires. Two things going wrong at the same moment, in two places, and only enough of me to put out one. To save the whole thing — the case, the rescue, the slow beautiful right-way plan I'd promised Ifeoma — I'd had to let one piece of it burn. And the piece I let burn was the corner of the board where Basti stood.

He hadn't argued. That was the unbearable part. When I told him to hold, when I told him I couldn't pull him back yet, that I needed him exactly where the danger was thickest because pulling him would unravel everything — he'd gone quiet for one second, and then he'd said, "Bon. Then I'll hold, fantôme. Just don't get clever and forget I'm here." And laughed. He'd laughed. And the laugh had landed in me like a stone going into deep water, down and down, no bottom.

So I did the only thing that lets a man keep functioning while someone he loves stands in a fire he lit: I got to work on the part I could control. I asked the system to show me the building.

I want to tell you what it gave me, and I want to do it without lying and without losing you, because this is the chapter where the place finally stops being a word — hospital, Marseille, the dead end of the dead end — and becomes a real thing with real rooms, one of which had Clara in it.

Picture this. You've stood outside a big dark building at night, haven't you, everyone has — a closed factory, an empty school, a hospital with its windows blind — and you've felt the way it refuses you. A building like that is a wall pretending to be a face. You can stare at it for an hour and learn nothing. It gives you brick and chained doors and the particular silence of a place where the lights went out years ago and never came back. From the outside, the old hospital on Basti's dead-end street was exactly that. A blank. A held breath made of concrete.

Here is the thing I'd built, told as plainly as I know how.

It could see past the face. It could read the bones.

Every building that has ever existed leaves a record of itself somewhere — in the papers filed when it was built, in the drawings made when it was changed, in the thousand small bureaucratic ghosts a structure sheds over its life the way a person sheds receipts and tickets and the stubs of old days. A hospital especially. A hospital is one of the most documented objects human beings make, because people are born and die in it and the law wants to know exactly where. Somewhere out in the world's vast forgotten paper, the whole skeleton of that building still existed — every floor, every corridor, every stairwell, every room and what it had once been for.

And the system went and found it. All of it. Pulled the scattered ghosts of a dead hospital out of the world's forgotten drawers, the way it pulled everything, patient and bloodless, and laid them down one over another until the blank face on Basti's street turned to glass in front of me and I could see straight through it into its own ribs.

I won't tell you how. I made you that promise on the first night and I'm keeping it on every night since. But I'll tell you what it felt like, because the feeling is the only part that's yours.

It felt like being given X-ray eyes for the one building on earth I needed them for.

The hospital had six floors.

I sat with that for a moment, just the number, the way you sit with a stranger's age. Six floors and a basement, built in a confident century that believed in itself, abandoned in a tired one that didn't. It had been, in its life, the kind of place a whole neighborhood was born in. There was a maternity wing on the third floor. I noticed that and wished I hadn't. There were wards and there were operating rooms and there was a chapel, a small one, tucked at the end of the east corridor on the ground floor for the families of people who weren't going to walk out. There was a morgue in the basement, because of course there was, and I'm telling you that detail not to be grim but because it mattered later in a way I couldn't have guessed, sitting there at three in the morning teaching myself a dead man's building.

I started at the top and worked down, the way you'd read a face from the hairline.

Sixth floor: nothing. Old administration, offices, the rooms where a hospital does its forgetting — files, ledgers, the rubber-stamp end of human suffering. Stripped, the drawings said, before the place was even chained. Whatever had been worth taking was long gone. Dead space.

Fifth, the same. Fourth, the same — wards, the long sad rooms where rows of beds had once stood, where now there'd be nothing but the marks on the floor where the beds had bolted down, like the chalk outline of a whole sleeping crowd.

Third floor: the maternity wing, and I made myself read it slowly, every room, because somewhere in the back of my skull a voice that sounded like Ifeoma was reminding me to read the whole thing and not just the part I could bear. Birthing rooms. A nursery. A long room with a wall of windows where, a hundred years of mornings, new mothers had held new people up to the first light they'd ever see. Now it was a dead room behind a chained door on a street with new chains on it. You don't put new chains on the dead, Basti had said. I was beginning to understand exactly what he meant, and exactly how much I was going to hate understanding it.

Because here's the thing about a skeleton. A skeleton tells you how a body is supposed to move. And when you lay the skeleton of a building over what you know is happening inside it — when you stop seeing brick and start seeing bones — you stop asking what is this building and you start asking the only question that ever matters in a place like that.

Where would I keep someone, if I were them?

I called Yuri, because that's exactly the kind of question Yuri was built to answer, and because I needed a voice in the room that wasn't mine and wasn't afraid the way mine was afraid.

He answered on the second ring, which for Yuri is practically a warm embrace. The hiss came down the line first, the little intake of breath that means he's already turning your problem over and finding the sharp edge of it before you've finished handing it to him.

"You found the inside," he said. Not a question. He could hear it in how I was breathing.

"Six floors," I said. "A basement. I'm trying to think like them. Where they'd put her."

A long pause. I heard him light something. I heard, very faintly, the eternal Kyiv soundscape behind him — a far siren, a kettle, the particular hush of a man who has not opened his curtains in two years.

"Not the top," he said. "Amateurs use the top. Top is a cage, yes, but a cage with one door and a long fall, and a clever prisoner with patience can do a great deal with a long fall and a great deal of patience. They are not amateurs." Another draw on the cigarette. "Not the wards. Too open. Too many windows. A person at a window is a person the world can see, and these people built their whole life around no one seeing anything."

"So."

"So they go down," said Yuri, and I felt the cold of it before he finished. "Down is where you put a thing you do not want found. Down has no windows. Down has thick walls and one stair and the kind of silence that eats a scream before it reaches the street. If I wanted to keep a person — truly keep her, keep her for a long time, keep her while the world looked and found nothing — I would put her under the ground in a building everyone agrees is dead." A beat. "And I would chain the doors above her with new chains, and trust that no one in the world could see past brick."

"Someone can," I said.

"Yes." I heard something in his voice I almost never heard. Not hope. Yuri doesn't do hope. Something colder and steadier than hope, and worth more. "That, my friend, is the part they did not plan for. They built a building no one could see into. And you built a thing that sees into buildings." The hiss again, softer. "Be careful what you do with eyes like that. A man who can see through walls forgets that walls are also the only thing keeping the roof off his head."

I didn't know what he meant. I would. Yuri is paranoid and Yuri is usually right and the gap between those two facts is exactly the size of the trouble you're about to be in.

I went down into the basement.

Not in person — I want to be clear, I was still in Brussels, still in a dead apartment with a dead clock, eight hundred miles from the salt and the rats and my friend standing in the dark. But the system took me down, the way it had taken me through every floor, and the basement was where the skeleton finally told me something true.

The basement was a maze.

The upper floors had been simple — long honest corridors, rooms in tidy rows, the architecture of a place that wanted to be found and used. But hospitals grow downward in a different way. Down in the dark, over a hundred years, they accrete. Boiler rooms. Storage. The morgue. Tunnels — actual tunnels, the drawings said, running out under the street to a wing that no longer existed, sealed now, dead ends in the dead end. A laundry the size of a cathedral. Rooms with no marked purpose at all, the kind of rooms a building forgets it has. And one space, near the heart of it, that the oldest drawings labeled in a careful dead hand as records. A vault, almost. Thick walls. One door. No windows because there was nothing for a window to look at but earth.

I looked at that room for a long time.

A room with thick walls and one door, in the lowest part of a building no one could see into, on a dead-end street in a city that had agreed to forget the whole block existed. A room built, a century ago, to keep paper safe from fire and damp and theft. To keep records.

The evidence is in the mesh, I thought, the way Clara always said it, the way she'd said it in my head every hour for nineteen days. Find the genome. And I understood, sitting there with the bones of a dead hospital glowing on my screen, that I'd been picturing her in the wrong kind of room this whole time. I'd pictured a cell. A cage. A frightened woman in a locked place, waiting to be saved.

But Clara hadn't gone into that building to be a prisoner. I knew that now, knew it the way you know a thing in your chest before your head will let you say it. Clara was a cryptographer who'd spent her whole brave exacting life on one idea — that the only real weapon left in a world of liars is the power to prove what's true. She hadn't let herself be taken into a cage.

She'd let herself be taken into a vault.

She'd walked into the one room in that whole dead building where the records were kept — where, I'd have bet my life on it, the records were still being kept, because a network that hides children inside charity needs somewhere to do its terrible bookkeeping, somewhere thick-walled and windowless and forgotten — and she'd planted herself in the middle of it, the smartest operator I'd ever known, and she'd been doing from the inside the exact thing I'd been doing from outside.

Bleeding the truth out, drop by drop, where it could be sealed and saved.

My hands were shaking. I put them flat on the desk to stop them. It didn't work.

Basti's voice came through low, and the music was gone out of it again, the way it had gone the first night.

"Fantôme. You still with me?"

"I'm here," I said. "I see it now. The inside. I see where she is."

"Tell me you see a way to her that doesn't go through the front, because the front is watched, my friend. I've been holding this corner like you asked and I will keep holding it, but I'll tell you what I'm looking at. I'm looking at a dead building and a car that is not dead, parked where no car should be, and a man in it who has not slept and is not bored, and a man who is not bored at three in the morning outside an empty hospital is a man being paid."

"Don't move," I said. "Whatever you see. Don't move toward it."

"I'm not moving." A breath. The harbor wind came through wherever he was, I could hear it tugging at the phone. "But fantôme — you remember what I said. New chains on a dead building. I keep thinking about it. The whole street is dead. The whole block. The streetlights here gave up years ago; the city forgot to keep paying the man who remembers to fix them. It is the darkest street in Marseille on purpose." He paused, and I heard him lean forward, the way he does when a thing has finally caught his eye and won't let go. "So tell me why."

"Why what?"

"Why," said Basti slowly, "the building is dark on every floor — every floor, fantôme, all of it black, dead as the moon —"

And I was already looking. Already pulling the skeleton back up, already laying the dead drawing of the place over whatever the system was quietly telling me about the living thing underneath it, the difference between how the building was supposed to be and how it actually, secretly, right now, was. And the answer rose up out of the dark and the dead floors with a cold I felt all the way to my teeth.

The hospital was dead on every floor but one.

Somewhere down in those bones, behind the new chains and the dead street and the man being paid not to be bored, a single floor of an abandoned hospital had power running to it. Light. Warmth. A heartbeat of current in a corpse that had been cold for years.

Someone was keeping the lights on.

For something.

Chapter 22: The Marketplace

The hospital had power on one floor only, and someone was keeping the lights on for something, and I could not stop thinking about what that something was.

I sat in the dark of a borrowed flat above a closed bakery in Marseille — the whole building smelled of cold flour and the ghost of bread — and I stared at the thought until it wore a groove in me. One floor. One window of light in a dead building, burning all night, fed by a generator someone had carried up four flights of stairs and bolted to the concrete and filled with fuel that cost money. You don't do that for nothing. You don't keep one room alive in a corpse of a building unless that room is worth more than the building. Unless that room is the only thing in the whole rotting place that still matters.

Clara was in that room. I knew it the way you know your own name in the dark.

But knowing where someone is and knowing why are two different animals, and the gap between them had been eating me for two days. They were keeping her alive. They were keeping a single floor of a forgotten hospital lit and warm and powered for her. And the thing I could not make fit — the splinter under the nail of everything — was that captors do not do that. Captors do not carry generators up four flights of stairs for a hostage. You don't invest in someone you intend to throw away.

So I stopped looking at the building.

And I started looking at the money.

Let me tell you about the money, because the money is where this whole story finally turned, and because I want you to understand it the way I came to understand it that night — not as numbers, but as a river.

Picture a river of mercy.

It starts clean. It starts as kindness — real kindness, from real people, good people in good cities who saw children in a charity's bright photographs and gave what they could. A pensioner in Lyon. A schoolteacher in Geneva who skipped a holiday. A man in Brussels who'd lost his own daughter young and gave every year on her birthday because giving was the only prayer he had left. That's where the river begins. In love. I want you to hold onto that, because it's the cruellest part: the river of this horror began, every single time, in somebody's love.

And then the river goes underground.

That's the only way I can say it that's true. The money these good people gave, the coins of their kindness, went into the charity and then it went under, into the dark place where rivers go when somebody wants them not to be seen. And down there in the dark, the river did the trick that's at the rotten heart of all of this — the trick that let these people sleep at night and stand in front of cameras and accept their awards. Down there, in the dark, mercy turned into cargo. The money that went in as help these children came out the other side as move these children, and nobody in the daylight ever saw the place where one became the other.

There is a marketplace in the dark. I'm not being poetic. There is an actual place — a hidden bazaar, a black country with no flag, where the people who live in the seams of the world buy and sell the things that can't be bought and sold in the light. I've been there. In my old life I walked those streets like a regular. I knew the stalls and the fences and the men who'd sell you anything if you had the coin and asked in the right voice. It's the back room of the whole planet, and most people will live their entire lives ten centimetres from its door and never know it's there.

That's where the river surfaced again. In the marketplace. That's where the money went to turn from mercy into cargo, and that's where — if I followed it, if I had the nerve to walk those streets again — it would tell me, finally, the truth of what I was looking at.

So I sent the tool down into the dark to follow the money.

I won't tell you how. You know I won't. I made you a promise on the first page and I'd sooner cut my own hand off than break it on the night it mattered most.

But I'll tell you what it was like, because that's the part I'm allowed to give you, and it's the part that matters anyway.

It was like setting a hound on a scent that ran through a city of ghosts.

The tool I'd built — the thing I made to find her, the thing my whole crooked life had been a rehearsal for — it could go where I no longer could. It could walk the streets of that hidden marketplace without a body to be hurt, without a face to be remembered, without a name anyone could put a price on. And it could do the one thing I'd never been patient enough to do in my breaking days: it could follow. Not crash in. Not burn it down. Just follow. One coin, through one door, into the next room, and the next, and the next, never losing the scent, never blinking, never tiring, all night long, down corridors of the dark world that branched and branched and branched.

I gave it the river. I gave it the place where the money went under. And then I sat in the dark above the bakery and I let it hunt, and I waited, the way you wait for a verdict, the way you wait outside a room where someone you love is being operated on. Hours. The cold flour smell. The generator-thought, four floors up, the one lit window I couldn't stop seeing.

And one by one, all night, it brought me back what it found. Not loudly. The tool never shouted. It just laid each thing at my feet, patient and certain and bloodless, the way it always did — here is a thread, and here is where it goes, and here is the seal that proves no one touched it but you.

Halfway through the night, Basti came up the stairs with two coffees and the careful quiet of a man who knows when not to talk. Basti is my locksmith in Marseille, my fixer, my boots on the ground, the smuggler who got tired of smuggling people and decided he'd rather open doors than break them. He has a laugh that fills a room and a heart he keeps hidden behind it like a knife behind a smile, and he's the only one of my family brave enough to sit with me in the worst hours and say nothing at all.

He put the coffee down. He looked at the dark, at the way the work was breathing in it, and he understood without being told that I was deep in the marketplace, walking streets he'd once walked too, in his own old life, before either of us learned that the right way costs more and is worth it.

"You're down in the bazaar," he said.

"I'm down in the bazaar."

He was quiet a moment. Then, low: "Be careful down there, brother. You know what that place does to people. It remembers everybody who ever walked it. You go in looking for one thing, and it shows you a hundred things you wish you'd never seen, and not all of them are about her." He squeezed my shoulder once, hard, the way he does instead of the words he can't say. "Whatever you find — it'll be true. That place doesn't lie. It just doesn't care whether the truth breaks you."

Then he went and sat by the window and watched the lit room across the city so I wouldn't have to, and I went back down into the dark.

By the small hours, the tool had walked the whole river to its mouth. It had followed the mercy of a pensioner in Lyon all the way down into the dark and out the other side, and it laid the map of that journey in front of me, and I looked at it, and the floor of the world dropped out from under me.

Because the marketplace told me three things, and each one was worse than the last.

The first thing was the scale. I'd known the number — two thousand eight hundred and forty-seven separate movements of money, each one a kindness when you looked at it alone, a confession when you stacked them up. But knowing a number and walking the river are different. Walking it, I felt the weight of it. Two thousand eight hundred and forty-seven times, somebody's love had been picked up in the dark and turned into freight. Two thousand eight hundred and forty-seven times. I had to stand up and walk to the window and put my forehead against the cold glass, because a number that size, when you finally feel it, doesn't sit in your head. It sits in your stomach.

The second thing was the names. The river didn't run wild. Rivers don't. It ran where the banks let it run, and the banks had names — twelve of them, the same twelve faces I'd already sealed, the philanthropists and the officials and the men who held children's hands for photographs. But walking the money showed me how they fit, how each one stood at a particular bend in the river and took his cut as it passed, and I saw for the first time that this wasn't twelve crooks who happened to know each other. It was a machine. A single machine with twelve hands, built carefully, over years, by someone patient. And I felt, at the edge of the map, beyond the twelve, the cold suggestion of a thirteenth shape — a hand the river bent around that the tool couldn't name, a bigger shadow the twelve were only the fingers of. I filed it away with a chill. Later. There's a later, and it's worse, and it's not for tonight. Tonight the twelve were enough.

And there was something else the river showed me, near the end, that I didn't understand yet and so I set it aside, the way you set aside a stone in your shoe when you're running for your life — you feel it, you mean to deal with it, you keep going.

It was this. The river had a guard. Not a fence, not a broker — a guard. Somewhere along its banks, in the daylight world this time, somebody with a badge and a clean reputation and a desk in a building full of good people was watching the river for the network. Watching to make sure it was never followed. And every time a hound got too close — every time some honest investigator in some honest country started up the bank toward the truth — a quiet word went out, and the trail went cold, and the investigator was moved to another case, or the file was lost, or the warrant came too late by exactly the margin that mattered. The river had a friend in the house of the law. I could feel the shape of him in the data the way you feel a draft and know a door is open somewhere you can't see. I couldn't name him yet. But I knew, with a cold certainty that settled into my chest and stayed there, that he was real, that he was close, that he was someone who wore the badge of the people I was about to trust my whole case to — and that before this was over I was going to have to look at his face. I filed it next to the thirteenth shadow. Later. There's a later.

And then the river reached its mouth, the place where it finally surfaced and the cargo changed hands, and the tool followed the very last coin into the very last room, and that's where it found the third thing.

The third thing was a door I wasn't supposed to find.

There was a room at the end of the river that didn't belong.

Every other room in that hidden bazaar was exactly what you'd expect — fences, brokers, the cold machinery of buying and selling people. But the last room, the deepest one, the one where the most protected secrets of PHANTOM MERCY were kept — that room was different. That room was locked. Not the way the others were locked. The others were locked the way a shop is locked, against thieves. This one was locked the way a vault is locked, against the world. Whatever was in that last room was the thing these people would burn everything else to protect. The crown jewel. The true ledger. The one set of files that named every name and mapped every route and proved every crime — the thing that, if it ever reached the daylight, would end them all forever.

And the tool, patient and certain and bloodless, brought me back the strangest thing of the whole night.

That last door — the deepest, most protected door in the entire rotten machine — had been touched. From the inside. Recently. Somebody was in there. Somebody had gotten past the lock that was built against the world, and was sitting inside the vault that held the end of PHANTOM MERCY, quietly, carefully, doing something to the files. Not stealing them. Not burning them. Copying them. Sealing them, one by one, the slow exacting way, pressing into each one a particular impossible mark so that years from now a stranger in a courtroom could pick it up and know it was true — nothing added, nothing removed, nothing tampered with in the dark.

I knew that mark.

I knew it the way you know a handwriting. I'd spent six days following that exact, patient, unbreakable way of doing things across the whole dark surface of the world — drop by drop, thread by thread, evidence bled into the seams of the planet by someone who refused, even at the end of everything, to do it any way but the right way.

It was her way.

It was Clara.

She was inside the vault. The most protected room in the entire network — the one door no captor would ever let a hostage near, the one place an outsider could never, ever reach — and she was inside it, working, calm, sealing the truth one piece at a time, building the unbreakable case from the very centre of the machine that had her.

And that's when the floor dropped out of me a second time, the worse time, the time it didn't come back.

Because you cannot get into that room as a prisoner. You cannot be taken into the heart of a thing like this. They would never let you near it. The only way — the only way — into the deepest room of the marketplace is to be trusted. To be one of them. To be carried in, smiling, as a colleague, through every lock that's built against the world, because you are not the world. You're family.

I sat in the dark above the bakery with the river spread out in front of me and the one lit window burning four floors up across the city, and I finally understood the thing that had been eating me for two days. The generator. The single floor kept alive. The investment they would never make in a hostage.

They didn't carry that generator up the stairs for a prisoner.

Clara had not been taken.

She walked in — on purpose — to reach something only an insider could touch.

Chapter 23: The Mole

Clara walked into the marketplace on purpose.

I'd spent a whole night with that and I still couldn't hold it without my hands shaking. She hadn't been taken. She'd walked in — chin up, spine a line, the way she walked toward every problem she was already three moves into solving — because there was a thing inside that beast that only an insider could touch, and she'd decided that the price of touching it was her own body, and she'd paid it without asking anyone's permission, least of all mine.

That's who she was. That's the woman I'd lost myself trying to find. She didn't get rescued. She infiltrated. And somewhere in the cold machinery of the dark, she was still in there, still bleeding the truth out drop by drop, still betting her life on a single proposition: that the man who loved her would be smart enough, and brave enough, and clean enough, to take what she handed him and finish it the right way.

But she'd left me one instruction, hadn't she. One line in the dead drop I'd been carrying around like a stone in my chest for two weeks.

Don't trust Station Marseille.

I'd read it a hundred times and each time I'd flinched past it, the way you flinch past the one sentence in a letter you don't have the strength to believe. Because Station Marseille wasn't an abstraction to me. It wasn't a building, a flag, a desk. It was a person. It was a voice. It was a face I'd eaten dinner across from, laughed with until the wine was gone, trusted with the kind of secrets you only ever tell people you've decided to die alongside.

I'd been refusing to ask the question for two weeks.

That night, finally, I asked it.

Let me tell you how it works, the not-trusting. Because I'd done it before — I'd done it in this very book, in a grey Brussels room, and it had nearly killed me — and you'd think it gets easier the second time.

It gets worse.

The first time, I asked the system whether someone was lying to me, and it said yes, and it didn't give me a name, and I told you that the no-name was a mercy I'd be grateful for. I lied. The no-name wasn't a mercy. It was a sentence with the verdict left blank, and I'd carried that blank for two weeks the way you carry a diagnosis you haven't booked the second appointment for. As long as there's no name, everyone's still innocent. As long as there's no name, you can still sit at the table with all of them and pretend the rot is somewhere else, in someone else, out in the dark where it belongs.

A name closes that door forever.

So I sat in the dark — a different dark now, a Marseille dark, because I'd come south at last, because you can't pull someone out of a fire from another country — and I looked at the phone on the desk and I thought about not asking. I thought about it hard. I am a coward in exactly one direction and that direction is the people I love. I will walk into any building. I will stare down any monster. But ask me to look at a friend and say prove to me you're not the one, and I become a child hiding under a coat, hoping the thunder is somebody else's storm.

But Clara walked in on purpose.

If she could walk into the marketplace with her chin up, knowing what they did to people in there, then I could walk into the one room I'd been refusing to enter — the small, ordinary, human room where you find out the worst thing about somebody you love. She'd spent her courage on the children. The least I could spend mine on was a question.

I asked the system to tell me who was lying.

Not whether. I knew whether. I'd known for two weeks. I asked it: who.

I won't tell you how it does it. You know that by now; it's the oldest promise in this book and I'm not breaking it tonight of all nights. But I'll tell you what it felt like to wait for the answer, because that's the part that's mine to give.

It felt like the moment after you've thrown something fragile and before it lands. That stretched, ringing nothing where the whole world holds still and you'd give anything — anything — to reach out and catch it, and you can't, because you're the one who threw it, and now you just have to stand there and watch it find the floor.

The system doesn't agonise. I've told you that. It doesn't hope, doesn't flinch, doesn't lie awake. It takes the warm braid of voices you've been leaning on, and it lays each one next to the cold quiet record the world keeps without meaning to, and it looks for the place where the story peels apart from the truth. It does this without love. That's its terrible gift and its terrible cruelty both. It loves no one, so it can see everyone clearly, which is the one thing love makes impossible.

I'd given it the braid. All of them. Every voice that had called me brother or love in the last two weeks. Every I was here, I was there, I couldn't reach you, I was dark, I didn't see the message. And one face more than the rest, one I'd added last, with my own hand shaking on the keys, the way you sign a thing you pray will come back blank.

The face from Station Marseille.

The liaison. The friend on the inside. The one human being in the entire machinery of the law I had let all the way in.

I want you to understand who that was before I tell you the answer, because otherwise the answer is just a plot, and it wasn't a plot, it was an amputation.

Let me tell you about her.

I'll call her by the name I called her, which was never her rank and never her title. Camille. Camille Reyer, INTERPOL liaison out of the Marseille station, the bridge between the world of badges and the world of ghosts — and the only person who had ever stood with one foot in each and not despised the other foot. The cops thought she was too close to the underworld. The underworld thought she was too close to the cops. Both were right, and that exact double-suspicion was why she was the best, and it was why I'd loved her the moment I met her, because it was the loneliest place in the world to stand and she'd been standing there for twenty years and never once asked anyone to feel sorry for her.

I'd met her four years ago, before Clara, before any of this, when I was still mostly the man who broke things and she was the cop who could've put me away and chose — for reasons she never fully explained — to put me to use instead. She'd looked across an interview table at a younger, wilder version of me, a man bleaking arrogance to cover the terror, and instead of the cage she'd offered me a chair. You're going to keep doing the thing you do, she'd said, lighting a cigarette she wasn't allowed to light in that room. I can't stop you. Nobody can stop a man who isn't afraid of losing what he has, because you've already decided you have nothing. So here's what I'll do instead. I'll point you. And she'd pointed me at worse men than me, for four years, and somewhere in there she stopped being my handler and became my friend, and somewhere after that she became something I don't have a clean word for — the older sister I'd never had, the conscience that came with a gun and a sense of humour blacker than mine.

She'd cried at no one's funeral and laughed at everyone's. She kept a photograph of a daughter she never spoke to in a locket she never took off. She'd talked me down from the dirty way more times than Ifeoma had, and she'd done it not with prayer but with the flat unanswerable arithmetic of a woman who'd watched a hundred vigilantes turn into the thing they hunted. Burn them, she'd say, and you save no one and you lose yourself, and then I have to be the one who hunts you, and I'm too old to hunt a man I like.

When Clara vanished, Camille was the first call I made that wasn't to my own circle. When I needed the law's standing without the law's chains, it was Camille who built me the bridge. When I came south, it was Camille who had a flat ready, a clean phone, a car, a route through the city that the bad people didn't watch. Every door I'd walked through in Marseille, she'd unlocked. Every step toward Clara, she'd lit.

She was the one person in this whole story I had stopped checking.

You check everyone, in the breaking life. You check the man who watches the street and the woman who carries the bag and the voice that says go. But there's always one. There's always one you decide, somewhere along the way, that you'd rather die than suspect, because to suspect them would cost more than the dying. Camille was mine. I'd handed her things I'd never handed Marchetti. I'd handed her the shape of the dead drop. I'd handed her, God help me, the timing of the move on Clara.

So when I added her face to the braid that night, last, with my hand shaking, I was already begging.

Come back blank, I told the dark. Come back blank. Let it be anyone. Let it be a stranger. Let it be the Surgeon wearing a friend's coat. Let it be Marchetti, even — let it be the cold hand of the state I never trusted anyway. But not her. Anyone but her.

The system doesn't take requests.

It took the braid and it did the lonely thing, the bird-at-your-feet thing, the thing it does better than any human who ever lived: it looked at the story and the truth side by side, without love, without kindness, without flinching, and it found the place where they peeled apart.

It didn't take long. It never takes long. That's the cruelty I keep coming back to — that the worst news of your life arrives with no ceremony, no drumroll, no chance to brace. One moment you're a man praying at a screen. The next moment the screen has answered, flatly, completely, the way a coroner answers, and the answer is sitting in the room with you and it will be sitting in the room with you for the rest of your life.

Most of the braid held. I want to say that, because it matters, because it meant my family was clean — Ifeoma exactly where she said, watching my back from Lagos with her coughing generator and her sleeping daughter; Basti exactly where he said, doing the boots-on-the-ground work in the streets he'd been born in; Yuri paranoid and dark and honest; Noa picking up the way she always picked up, owing me nothing and giving it anyway. The rope held. The people I'd led into the fire had not lit it. I felt that relief land like a blessing I hadn't earned, and I had about one second to feel it.

Because one thread ran the wrong way.

One voice had told me a place, and the world had quietly written down a different place. One mouth had said I was with the station all night on the night the marker moved, and the world remembered the station empty and the lights off and a car — her car, the one she'd given me a twin of, the one nobody watched — out in the dark on a road that led exactly one direction. One person had taken the shape of the dead drop from my own hand and carried it somewhere it should never have gone. One person had known the timing of the move on Clara, and one person had let the timing slip into the dark, and Clara had walked into a marketplace that was waiting for her — because someone had told it she was coming.

I sat there and I felt the floor go out from under me for the last time in this book, and it went slow, the way the worst ones always do, a developing photograph, grey water remembering it was a face.

And underneath the grief — and it was grief, I won't dress it up, it was the same animal sound you make at a graveside — there was a second thing, colder, that I hated myself for feeling so fast. A tactician's thing. Because the moment the system named the leak, it changed every plan I had. I had been days from going to Clara. I had a route, a window, a way in — and every piece of it had passed through Camille's hands, which meant every piece of it was already in the dark, already known, already waiting for me the way the marketplace had waited for Clara. If I moved now, I'd be moving into a room they'd had time to fill. The rescue I'd built was poisoned at the root, and the person who'd poisoned it was the person I'd built it with.

There was a wrong way to use what I now knew. I felt that one too, quick and hot. I could call Camille, right now, at this charming hour, and I could let her hear in my voice that I knew, and I could watch her world end the way she'd helped end mine. I could turn the dark on her. I could make her afraid.

But that's the loud thing, and the loud thing saves no one. If she knew I knew, she'd run, or worse — she'd tell them I knew, and they'd move Clara, or end Clara, before I could get within a mile of her. The only thing I had left, the only edge in the whole black world, was that the mole did not yet know she'd been found.

Which meant I had to do the hardest piece of acting of my life. I had to keep loving her to her face. I had to take her calls and use her flat and walk through her unlocked doors and let her light the way to Clara — knowing every light she lit was a trap — and I had to build, in secret, a different way in, one she'd never touch, one I'd rehearse in the dark with the only people I knew were clean, until it was perfect, until it was the one true door in a building full of false ones. I had to use the traitor's own lit path as the bait, and walk a path she couldn't see.

I'd have to practise it until I couldn't get it wrong. Because you only get one walk through a door that's waiting for you, and the difference between a rescue and a funeral is the rehearsal nobody films.

That was tomorrow's problem. Tomorrow's, and the family's — the clean ones, the ones who'd held.

Tonight there was only the name.

I didn't want to look at the name.

I made myself look at the name.

It was the friend who'd offered me a chair instead of a cage. It was the locket and the daughter she never spoke to. It was twenty years of standing in the loneliest place in the world. It was the older sister I'd never had, the conscience with the gun, the woman who'd talked me down from becoming a monster more times than I could count — and who, it turned out, had simply been a better one all along, hiding in the one place I would never think to look, which was the place I loved.

Camille was the mole.

Camille Reyer, Station Marseille, the one person in this whole story I would have trusted with my life.

Chapter 24: Rehearsal

The mole had a face now, and I couldn't make it stop being kind.

That was the cruelty of it. I'd spent the night before learning a name I would have bet my life on, a name I had bet my life on, more than once, in worse rooms than this one. And now I had to take that name and set it aside like a knife you can't put down because you might still need it, and turn my whole burning attention to a building in Marseille where Clara was running out of the time she was spending so bravely. Grief would have to wait. Betrayal would have to wait. Everything human in me would have to stand in a corner and be quiet while the cold part of me did the only thing that could still save her.

The cold part of me built a plan.

Or — and this is the truth I keep having to tell you, the one I promised at the start and won't take back now — the tool built it, and I sat behind it like a man behind a horse, holding reins I half understood, trusting it to see the ground I couldn't.

Here is what I needed it to do, in plain words.

I needed to get into that building. And before I sent a single living person through a single door, I needed to know what was on the other side of that door — every door, every window, every dark stairwell — without anyone touching it. I needed to walk the whole rescue, end to end, a hundred times, a thousand times, and die in all the ways I could die, so that the one time it happened with real flesh and real breath, it would be the time I'd already learned by heart.

I needed to rehearse.

Not with bodies. Not yet. With ghosts.

Let me tell you what that felt like, because it is the strangest thing I have ever done and I still dream about it.

I sat in the dark of a rented flat in Marseille — Basti had found it, three streets from the harbour, smelling of someone else's life — and I built the building inside the machine. Not a drawing. Not a map. A place. The system took everything it had bled out of the world over seven days — the old plans of a hospital that had been left to die, the way the power still ran to it like a pulse in a corpse, the cameras nobody had thought to switch off, the patient ghost of a heartbeat that was Clara, somewhere on a high floor — and it built me a model of that building so complete that I could move through it without leaving my chair.

And then it did the thing I will never be able to explain to my mother, though God knows I'll try.

It started killing me.

It ran the rescue. Over and over. It sent imagined men through the front, and watched them die at the front, in the cold flat arithmetic of the thing — here a camera sees you, here a door is locked from inside, here a man with a gun stands where you didn't expect a man with a gun. It sent them through the loading dock at the back, where the convoys used to swallow their cargo, and watched them die there too, slower. It sent them up the stairwells and down the lift shafts and across the roof, and each time it laid the failure at my feet without a flicker of feeling, patient as a tide: this way, you lose four. This way, you lose everyone. This way you reach the third floor and the door behind you locks and now you are the one in the cage.

I want you to understand what this is, because the films get it wrong. They show you the hero kicking the door, the slow-motion glass, the music. They never show you the part before, the part that actually saves the children — a man sitting very still in the dark, watching a machine murder his own imagination a thousand times, so that the real night will only have to be survived once.

Every way in, it tested. Every single way.

The front, where the old grand entrance still stood with its dead glass and its faded cross. Too open. Too watched. The system showed me the eyes on it — the cameras these people had left running, the cameras they watched from wherever they sat — and it showed me, cold and certain, that a man walking up those steps was a man already seen, already counted, already lost. No.

The harbour side, where the loading bays gaped. Better cover. Worse inside. The system walked me down those corridors and showed me where they narrowed, where a man could be funneled and finished, where the dark did the work and you never even saw the hand that did it. No.

The roof. The hopeful one, the cinematic one. I wanted it to be the roof so badly. The system let me dream it for exactly as long as it took to show me the single locked door at the bottom of the single stairwell, the door that turned the whole roof into a beautiful trap with a view. No.

Hours of this. The candle of the night burning down, and Clara on her high floor spending herself drop by drop, and me in the dark dying a thousand bloodless deaths so that I might learn the one path where nobody real had to.

Basti came in around two with bread and a bottle of something and the particular silence of a man who has seen me like this before and knows not to fill it.

Basti is the one who keeps me human when the machine is trying to make me into one of its own cold parts. A smuggler who became a locksmith, which is to say a man who spent the first half of his life learning how to open things that weren't his and the second half learning how to keep things safe for people who were afraid — Marseille in one body, the dirty harbour and the good heart, the joke at the funeral and the hand that digs the grave. He is the reason I laugh in rooms I should be weeping in. He has carried me, literally, more than once. If I have a brother, it is this loud thieving tender man who is, right now, tearing bread in the dark and pretending not to watch me drown.

"You've found the door," he said. Not a question. He could read the work on my face the way Ifeoma read it in the dark.

"I've found that there isn't one," I said. "Not a clean one. Every way in, somebody dies. The front, the back, the roof. It runs them all and they all end the same. I keep waiting for it to find the one I'm missing and it keeps finding the one I'm dreaming."

Basti chewed. Sat on the edge of the table the way he does, all his weight on a thing that shouldn't hold it. "So there's no way in."

"There's always a way in," I said, and I heard, with a small cold shock, that the man saying it was the old one. The one who broke things. The one who had stood outside enough impossible buildings to know that impossible is just a word people use for a door they haven't found yet. "There's always a way in, Basti. The dark always has a seam. You just have to be willing to look at the part of yourself you don't want to look at."

He stopped chewing. He's known me too long. "What does that mean, with your face doing that."

"It means," I said slowly, watching the machine the way you watch a card turn over, "that the way in isn't through the doors. The doors are watched because the doors are what people watch. The way in is through the people. Through the trust. Through the part of the building that lets the right man walk straight through the front of it because they think they know who he is."

I made the system do one more thing, then. The hardest thing I'd asked it all night. I told it to stop testing the building and start testing the people — to take everyone who could move freely in and out of that place, the suppliers, the cleaners, the men in the good lobbies who carried the lie like a credential, and to find me the one identity, the one face, the one role that could walk up to that dead grand entrance and have the watching eyes simply let it pass. Not break in. Walk in. Invited. Expected. Unremarkable.

It gathered. The candle burned. Basti stopped pretending to eat.

And then it laid the answer at my feet the way it always did — patient, bloodless, certain — and the cold part of me read it, and the human part of me went very still, because I understood before I'd finished reading exactly what it was going to cost.

I should tell you about the cost. Because this is the part of me that the bible of my whole crooked life keeps coming back to, the part Clara saw before anyone, the part she once said she'd marry if I ever let it win over the other parts.

There were two ways to use what the machine had just handed me.

There was the dirty way. There is always the dirty way; it lives in my hands and whispers at three in the morning and it is, God help me, usually the easy one. I could have taken that single role, that single trusted face, and become it the way I used to become things — slid into someone's life like a coat off a hook, wore their name and their welcome and their unremarkableness, and walked into that building as a lie so smooth not even the watching eyes would itch. I'd done it a hundred times in the years I'm not proud of. I was good at it. I was, if you want the ugly truth, great at it. Becoming someone trusted in order to walk through a door is the single thing my misspent life made me a master of, and the master was right here, in the dark, with the woman he loved on a high floor running out of time, and every reason in the world to pick the lock the old way.

And there was the right way. Clara's way. The slow way. The way that said: even now, even here, even to save her — you do not become a lie to do it. You do not put on another man's face. You do not let the old you out of the corner where he's been standing quiet all night, because the moment you do, you'll find you can't put him back, and then what did you save her for? A man who'll lie his way through any door is a man who'll lie his way through any door for the rest of his life, including the doors that lead back to her.

I sat with both. I will not pretend I didn't. I turned the dirty way over like a stone and felt the cool relief of its underside — just this once, just to reach her, who would even blame you — and then I set it down, the way I'd set down the mole's kind face, the way Clara had taught me to set down everything that felt like courage and was only noise.

There had to be a clean version. A role I could honestly be, not steal. A face the building would let through that I could put on without putting off my soul.

So I asked the machine for that. The narrow door. The one path in that was both possible and clean. Walk me in, I told it, the right way, or tell me there isn't one and I'll find another building entirely, another night, another plan, because I will not save her by becoming the thing she nearly died to stop.

It thought. The whole patient weight of it bent to the question. Basti watched me not breathe.

And then it found it. One role. One identity that could cross that threshold by daylight, expected, welcomed, waved through — and that I could honestly inhabit, because the seam it had found wasn't a lie I'd have to wear. It was a door from my own past. A connection that already, truly, existed — a thread from the life before, from the rooms I don't talk about, a real link between the man I used to be and the world that ran that building. No costume. No theft. Just a door my own history had already, long ago, cut into the wall.
